From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

(Redirected from [Mucube](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mucube&redirect=no "Mucube"))

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Mucube_internal.png/330px-Mucube_internal.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Mucube_internal.png)

The [mucube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mucube "Mucube") is a regular skew apeirohedron.

In [geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry "Geometry"), a **regular skew apeirohedron** is an infinite [regular skew polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_polyhedron "Regular skew polyhedron"). They have either [skew](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skew_polygon "Skew polygon") regular [faces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Face_\(geometry\) "Face (geometry)") or skew regular [vertex figures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_figure "Vertex figure").

## History

In 1926 [John Flinders Petrie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Flinders_Petrie "John Flinders Petrie") took the concept of [regular skew polygons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_polygon "Regular skew polygon"), polygons whose vertices are not all in the same plane, and extended it to polyhedra. While [apeirohedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_tiling "Regular tiling") are typically required to tile the 2-dimensional plane, Petrie considered cases where the faces were still convex but were not required to lie flat in the plane, they could have a skew polygon [vertex figure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_figure "Vertex figure").

Petrie discovered two regular skew apeirohedra, the mucube and the muoctahedron.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-M&Shistory-1) [Harold Scott MacDonald Coxeter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.S.M._Coxeter "H.S.M. Coxeter") derived a third, the mutetrahedron, and proved that these three were complete. Under Coxeter and Petrie's definition, requiring convex faces and allowing a skew vertex figure, the three were not only the only skew apeirohedra in 3-dimensional Euclidean space, but they were the only skew polyhedra in 3-space as there Coxeter showed there were no finite cases.

In 1967[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-2) Garner investigated regular skew apeirohedra in [hyperbolic 3-space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperbolic_3-space "Hyperbolic 3-space") with Petrie and Coxeters definition, discovering 31[[note 1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-miscount-3) regular skew apeirohedra with compact or paracompact symmetry.

In 1977[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-4)[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-M&Shistory-1) [Grünbaum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Branko_Gr%C3%BCnbaum "Branko Grünbaum") generalized skew polyhedra to allow for skew faces as well. Grünbaum discovered an additional 23[[note 2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-blend_count-5) skew apeirohedra in 3-dimensional Euclidean space and 3 in 2-dimensional space which are skew by virtue of their faces. 12 of Grünbaum's polyhedra were formed using the blending operation on 2-dimensional apeirohedra, and the other 11 were pure, i.e. could not be formed by a non-trivial blend. Grünbaum conjectured that this new list was complete for the parameters considered.

In 1985[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-6)[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-M&Shistory-1) Dress found an additional pure regular skew apeirohedron in 3-space, and proved that with this additional skew apeirohedron the list was complete.

## Regular skew apeirohedra in Euclidean 3-space

### Petrie-Coxeter polyhedra

The three Euclidean solutions in 3-space are {4,6|4}, {6,4|4}, and {6,6|3}. [John Conway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Horton_Conway "John Horton Conway") named them mucube, muoctahedron, and mutetrahedron respectively for multiple cube, octahedron, and tetrahedron.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-7)

1. **Mucube**: {4,6|4}: 6 [squares](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_\(geometry\) "Square (geometry)") about each vertex (related to [cubic honeycomb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cubic_honeycomb "Cubic honeycomb"), constructed by cubic cells, removing two opposite faces from each, and linking sets of six together around a faceless [cube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cube "Cube").)
2. **Muoctahedron**: {6,4|4}: 4 [hexagons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagon "Hexagon") about each vertex (related to [bitruncated cubic honeycomb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bitruncated_cubic_honeycomb "Bitruncated cubic honeycomb"), constructed by [truncated octahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_octahedron "Truncated octahedron") with their square faces removed and linking hole pairs of holes together.)
3. **Mutetrahedron**: {6,6|3}: 6 hexagons about each vertex (related to [quarter cubic honeycomb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quarter_cubic_honeycomb "Quarter cubic honeycomb"), constructed by [truncated tetrahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_tetrahedron "Truncated tetrahedron") cells, removing triangle faces, and linking sets of four around a faceless [tetrahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrahedron "Tetrahedron").)

Coxeter gives these regular skew apeirohedra {2q,2r|p} with [extended chiral symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coxeter_notation "Coxeter notation") [[(_p_,_q_,_p_,_r_)]+] which he says is isomorphic to his [abstract group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstract_group "Abstract group") (2_q_,2_r_|2,_p_). The related honeycomb has the extended symmetry [[(_p_,_q_,_p_,_r_)]].[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-8)

### Grünbaum-Dress polyhedra

#### Skew honeycombs

There are 3 regular skew apeirohedra of full rank, also called **regular skew honeycombs**, that is skew apeirohedra in 2-dimensions. As with the [finite skew polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_polyhedron#Finite_regular_skew_polyhedra "Regular skew polyhedron") of full rank, all three of these can be obtained by applying the [Petrie dual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petrie_dual "Petrie dual") to planar polytopes, in this case the three regular tilings.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-9)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-10)[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-11)

Alternatively they can be constructed using the apeir operation on regular polygons.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-12) While the Petrial is used the classical construction, it does not generalize well to higher ranks. In contrast, the apeir operation is used to construct higher rank skew honeycombs.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-13)

The apeir operation takes the generating mirrors of the polygon, ρ0 and ρ1, and uses them as the mirrors for the vertex figure of a polyhedron, the new vertex mirror w is then a point located where the initial vertex of the polygon (or anywhere on the mirror ρ1 other than its intersection with ρ0). The new initial vertex is placed at the intersection of the mirrors ρ0 and ρ1. Thus the apeir polyhedron is generated by ⟨w, ρ0, ρ0⟩.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-14)

|Skew honeycombs|[Schläfli symbol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schl%C3%A4fli_symbol "Schläfli symbol")|   |Faces|Image|Petrie dual|Apeir of|   |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
|Petrial square tiling|{4,4}π|{∞,4}4|∞ [zigzags](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zigzag "Zigzag")|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Squat_petp.svg/330px-Squat_petp.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Squat_petp.svg)|[Square tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_tiling "Square tiling")|[Square](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square "Square")|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Apeir_square.svg/250px-Apeir_square.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apeir_square.svg)|
|Petrial triangular tiling|{3,6}π|{∞,6}3|∞ [zigzags](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zigzag "Zigzag")|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Trat_petp.svg/330px-Trat_petp.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Trat_petp.svg)|[Triangular tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangular_tiling "Triangular tiling")|[Hexagon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagon "Hexagon")|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Apeir_hexagon.svg/250px-Apeir_hexagon.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apeir_hexagon.svg)|
|Petrial hexagonal tiling|{6,3}π|{∞,3}6|∞ [zigzags](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zigzag "Zigzag")|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Hexat_petp.svg/330px-Hexat_petp.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hexat_petp.svg)|[Hexagonal tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagonal_tiling "Hexagonal tiling")|[Triangle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangle "Triangle")|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Apeir_triangle.svg/250px-Apeir_triangle.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Apeir_triangle.svg)|

#### Blended apeirohedra

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Blended_triangular_tiling.png/500px-Blended_triangular_tiling.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Blended_triangular_tiling.png)

{3,6}#{} with the edges of one face highlighted in red

For any two regular polytopes, P and Q, a new polytope can be made by the following process:

- Start with the [Cartesian product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cartesian_product "Cartesian product") of the vertices of P with the vertices of Q.
- Add edges between any two vertices p0 × q0 and p1 × q1 if there is an edge between p0 and p1 in P and an edge between q0 and q1 in Q. (If Q has no edges then add a virtual edge connecting its vertex to itself.)
- Similarly add faces to every set of vertices all incident on the same face in both P and Q. (If Q has no faces then add a virtual face connecting its edge to itself.)
- Repeat as such for all ranks of proper elements.
- From the resulting polytope, select one connected component.

For regular polytopes the last step is guaranteed to produce a unique result. This new polytope is called the **blend** of P and Q and is represented P#Q.

Equivalently the blend can be obtained by positioning P and Q in orthogonal spaces and taking composing their generating mirrors pairwise.

Blended polyhedra in 3-dimensional space can be made by blending 2-dimensional polyhedra with 1-dimensional polytopes. The only 2-dimensional polyhedra are the 6 honeycombs (3 Euclidean tilings and 3 [skew honeycombs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#skew_honeycombs)):

- [Triangular tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangular_tiling "Triangular tiling"): {3, 6}
- [Square tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_tiling "Square tiling"): {4, 4}
- [Hexagonal tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagonal_tiling "Hexagonal tiling"): {6, 3}
- Petrial triangular tiling: {3, 6}π
- Petrial square tiling: {4, 4}π
- Petrial hexagonal tiling: {6, 3}π

The only 1-dimensional polytopes are:

- The [line segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Line_segment "Line segment"): {}
- The [apeirogon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apeirogon "Apeirogon"): {∞}

Each pair between these produces a valid distinct regular skew apeirohedron in 3-dimensional Euclidean space, for a total of 12[[note 2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-blend_count-5) blended skew apeirohedra.

Since the skeleton of the square tiling is [bipartite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bipartite_graph "Bipartite graph"), two of these blends, {4, 4}#{} and {4, 4}π#{}, are combinatrially equivalent to their non-blended counterparts.

#### Pure apeirohedra

![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Skew_apeirohedra_relations.svg/500px-Skew_apeirohedra_relations.svg.png)[](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Skew_apeirohedra_relations.svg)

Some relationships between the 12 pure apeirohedra in 3D Euclidean space

- π represents the [Petrial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petrial "Petrial")
- δ represents the [dual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dual_polyhedron "Dual polyhedron")
- η represents halving
- φ represents facetting
- σ represents skewing
- r represents [rectification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rectification_\(geometry\) "Rectification (geometry)")

|   |   |
|---|---|
|[![[icon]](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg/20px-Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Wiki_letter_w_cropped.svg)|This section **needs expansion**. You can help by [adding missing information](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Regular_skew_apeirohedron&action=edit&section=). _(February 2024)_|

A polytope is considered pure if it cannot be expressed as a non-trivial blend of two polytopes. A blend is considered trivial if it contains the result as one of the components. Alternatively a pure polytope is one whose symmetry group contains no non-trivial [subrepresentation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subrepresentation "Subrepresentation").[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-15)

There are 12 regular pure apeirohedra in 3 dimensions. Three of these are the [Petrie-Coxeter polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petrie-Coxeter_polyhedra "Petrie-Coxeter polyhedra"):

- {4,6 | 4}
- {6,4 | 4}
- {6,6 | 3}

Three more are obtained as the [Petrials](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petrial "Petrial") of the Petrie-Coxeter polyhedra:

- {4,6 | 4}π = {∞, 4}6,4
- {6,4 | 4}π = {∞, 6}4,4
- {6,6 | 3}π = {∞, 6}6,3

Three additional pure apeirohedra can be formed with finite skew polygons as faces:

- [![6 skew hexagons in a hexagonal arrangement form the vertex of {6,6}4.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/HMC_vertex.png/500px-HMC_vertex.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:HMC_vertex.png "6 skew hexagons in a hexagonal arrangement form the vertex of {6,6}4.")
    
    6 [skew hexagons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skew_hexagon "Skew hexagon") in a [hexagonal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagon "Hexagon") arrangement form the vertex of {6,6}4.
    
 - [![6 skew squares in a hexagonal arrangement form the vertex of {4,6}6.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/PHMC_vertex.svg/500px-PHMC_vertex.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:PHMC_vertex.svg "6 skew squares in a hexagonal arrangement form the vertex of {4,6}6.")
    
    6 skew squares in a [hexagonal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagon "Hexagon") arrangement form the vertex of {4,6}6.
    
 - [![4 skew hexagons in a square arrangement form the vertex of {6,4}6.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/SPMO_vertex.svg/500px-SPMO_vertex.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:SPMO_vertex.svg "4 skew hexagons in a square arrangement form the vertex of {6,4}6.")
    
    4 [skew hexagons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skew_hexagon "Skew hexagon") in a [square](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square "Square") arrangement form the vertex of {6,4}6.
    

These 3 are closed under the [Wilson operations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilson_operation "Wilson operation"). Meaning that each can be constructed from any other by some combination of the Petrial and dual operations. {6,6}4 is [self-dual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-dual_polyhedron "Self-dual polyhedron") and {6,4}6 is self-Petrial.

## Regular skew apeirohedra in hyperbolic 3-space

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Hyperbolic_skew_apeirohedron_4%2C6_5.png/500px-Hyperbolic_skew_apeirohedron_4%2C6_5.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hyperbolic_skew_apeirohedron_4,6_5.png)

The compact skew apeirohedron {4,6 | 5}

In 1967, C. W. L. Garner identified 31 hyperbolic skew apeirohedra with [regular skew polygon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_polygon "Regular skew polygon") [vertex figures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_figure "Vertex figure"), found by extending the [Petrie-Coxeter polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Petrie-Coxeter_polyhedra "Petrie-Coxeter polyhedra") to hyperbolic space.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-16)

These represent 14 compact and 17[[note 1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_skew_apeirohedron#cite_note-miscount-3) paracompact regular skew polyhedra in hyperbolic space, constructed from the symmetry of a subset of linear and cyclic [Coxeter groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coxeter_group "Coxeter group") graphs of the form [[(_p_,_q_,_p_,_r_)]], These define _regular skew polyhedra_ {2_q_,2_r_|_p_} and dual {2_r_,2_q_|_p_}. For the special case of linear graph groups _r_ = 2, this represents the Coxeter group [_p_,_q_,_p_]. It generates regular skews {2_q_,_4_|_p_} and {4,2_q_|_p_}. All of these exist as a subset of faces of the [convex uniform honeycombs in hyperbolic space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_uniform_honeycombs_in_hyperbolic_space "Convex uniform honeycombs in hyperbolic space").

The skew apeirohedron shares the same [antiprism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiprism "Antiprism") vertex figure with the honeycomb, but only the zig-zag edge faces of the vertex figure are realized, while the other faces make holes.

|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
14 Compact regular skew apeirohedra

## Notes

1.  Garner mistakenly counts {8,8|4} twice giving a count of 18 paracompact cases and 32 total, but only listing 17 paracompact and 31 total.
2.  Polytopes produced as a non-trivial blend have a degree of freedom corresponding to the relative scaling of their components. For this reason some authors count these as infinite families rather than a single polytope. This article counts two polytopes as equal when there is an [affine map](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Affine_map "Affine map") of [full rank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Full_rank "Full rank") between them.

---
# Truncated trihexagonal tiling

In [geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry "Geometry"), the **truncated trihexagonal tiling** is one of eight [semiregular tilings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_uniform_tilings "List of uniform tilings") of the Euclidean plane. There are one [square](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_\(geometry\) "Square (geometry)"), one [hexagon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagon "Hexagon"), and one [dodecagon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodecagon "Dodecagon") on each [vertex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_\(geometry\) "Vertex (geometry)"). It has [Schläfli symbol](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Schl%C3%A4fli_symbol "Schläfli symbol") of _tr_{3,6}.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Rhombic_truncated_trihexagonal_tiling.svg/250px-Rhombic_truncated_trihexagonal_tiling.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Rhombic_truncated_trihexagonal_tiling.svg)

An equilateral variation with rhombi instead of squares, and [isotoxal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotoxal_figure "Isotoxal figure") hexagons instead of regular

## Names

|   |   |
|---|---|
|The name _truncated trihexagonal tiling_ is analogous to _[truncated cuboctahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_cuboctahedron "Truncated cuboctahedron")_ and _[truncated icosidodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_icosidodecahedron "Truncated icosidodecahedron")_, and misleading in the same way. An actual [truncation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncation_\(geometry\) "Truncation (geometry)") of the [trihexagonal tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trihexagonal_tiling "Trihexagonal tiling") has rectangles instead of squares, and its hexagonal and dodecagonal faces can not both be regular.<br><br>Alternate interchangeable names are:<br><br>- Great rhombitrihexagonal tiling<br>- Rhombitruncated trihexagonal tiling<br>- Omnitruncated hexagonal tiling, omnitruncated triangular tiling<br>- [Conway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Horton_Conway "John Horton Conway") calls it a _truncated hexadeltille_.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_trihexagonal_tiling#cite_note-conway2008-1)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Tiling_3-6_simple.svg/250px-Tiling_3-6_simple.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tiling_3-6_simple.svg)<br><br>[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Tiling_3-6%2C_truncated.svg/250px-Tiling_3-6%2C_truncated.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tiling_3-6,_truncated.svg)<br><br>Trihexagonal tiling and its truncation|

## Uniform colorings

There is only one [uniform coloring](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uniform_coloring "Uniform coloring") of a truncated trihexagonal tiling, with faces colored by polygon sides. A 2-uniform coloring has two colors of hexagons. 3-uniform colorings can have 3 colors of dodecagons or 3 colors of squares.

||1-uniform|2-uniform|3-uniform|   |
|---|---|---|---|---|
|Coloring|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012.png/120px-Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012.png)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012b.png/120px-Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012b.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012b.png)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012c.png/120px-Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012c.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012c.png)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012d.png/120px-Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012d.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Uniform_polyhedron-63-t012d.png)|
|[Symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_planar_symmetry_groups#Wallpaper_groups "List of planar symmetry groups")|p6m, [6,3], (*632)|p3m1, [3[3]], (*333)|   |   |   |

## Related 2-uniform tilings

The _truncated trihexagonal tiling_ has three related [2-uniform tilings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2-uniform_tiling "2-uniform tiling"), one being a 2-uniform coloring of the semiregular [rhombitrihexagonal tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombitrihexagonal_tiling "Rhombitrihexagonal tiling"). The first dissects the hexagons into 6 triangles. The other two dissect the [dodecagons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodecagon "Dodecagon") into a central hexagon and surrounding triangles and square, in two different orientations.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_trihexagonal_tiling#cite_note-2)[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_trihexagonal_tiling#cite_note-3)

|Semiregular|Dissections|Semiregular|2-uniform|   |3-uniform|
|---|---|---|---|---|---|

## Circle packing

The Truncated trihexagonal tiling can be used as a [circle packing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circle_packing "Circle packing"), placing equal diameter circles at the center of every point. Every circle is in contact with 3 other circles in the packing ([kissing number](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kissing_number "Kissing number")).[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_trihexagonal_tiling#cite_note-Critchlow-4)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/1-uniform-3-circlepack.svg/250px-1-uniform-3-circlepack.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1-uniform-3-circlepack.svg)

## Kisrhombille tiling

|Kisrhombille tiling|   |
|---|---|
|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Tiling_great_rhombi_3-6_dual_simple.svg/250px-Tiling_great_rhombi_3-6_dual_simple.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tiling_great_rhombi_3-6_dual_simple.svg)|   |
|Type|[Dual semiregular tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_uniform_tilings "List of uniform tilings")|
|[Coxeter diagram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coxeter_diagram "Coxeter diagram")|![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9b/CDel_node_f1.png)![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c3/CDel_3.png)![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9b/CDel_node_f1.png)![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/32/CDel_6.png)![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9b/CDel_node_f1.png)|
|[Wallpaper group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wallpaper_group "Wallpaper group")|p6m, [6,3], (*632)|
|[Rotation group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point_groups_in_three_dimensions#Rotation_groups "Point groups in three dimensions")|p6, [6,3]+, (632)|
|[Dual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dual_polyhedron "Dual polyhedron")|truncated trihexagonal tiling|
|[Face configuration](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Face_configuration "Face configuration")|V4.6.12[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Tiling_great_rhombi_3-6_dual_face.svg/60px-Tiling_great_rhombi_3-6_dual_face.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tiling_great_rhombi_3-6_dual_face.svg)|
|Properties|[face-transitive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Face-transitive "Face-transitive")|

The **kisrhombille tiling** or **3-6 kisrhombille tiling** is a tiling of the Euclidean plane. It is constructed by congruent [30-60-90 triangles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/30_60_90 "30 60 90") with 4, 6, and 12 triangles meeting at each vertex.

Subdividing the faces of these tilings creates the kisrhombille tiling. (Compare the disdyakis [hexa-](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrakis_hexahedron#Symmetry "Tetrakis hexahedron"), [dodeca-](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disdyakis_dodecahedron#Symmetry "Disdyakis dodecahedron") and [triacontahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disdyakis_triacontahedron#Symmetry "Disdyakis triacontahedron"), three [Catalan solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalan_solid "Catalan solid") similar to this tiling.)

- [![3-6 deltoidal](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Kisrhombille_in_deltoidal.svg/120px-Kisrhombille_in_deltoidal.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kisrhombille_in_deltoidal.svg "3-6 deltoidal")
    
    [3-6 deltoidal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deltoidal_trihexagonal_tiling "Deltoidal trihexagonal tiling")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Kisrhombille_under_truncated_trihexagonal.svg/250px-Kisrhombille_under_truncated_trihexagonal.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kisrhombille_under_truncated_trihexagonal.svg)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Kisrhombille_under_floret_pentagonal_left_%28light_faces%29.svg/250px-Kisrhombille_under_floret_pentagonal_left_%28light_faces%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Kisrhombille_under_floret_pentagonal_left_\(light_faces\).svg)

The kisrhombille tiling under its dual (left) and under the [floret pentagonal tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Floret_pentagonal_tiling "Floret pentagonal tiling") (right), from which it can be created as a [partial truncation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncation_\(geometry\)#Alternation_or_partial_truncation "Truncation (geometry)").

### Construction from rhombille tiling

[Conway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Horton_Conway "John Horton Conway") calls it a **kisrhombille**[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_trihexagonal_tiling#cite_note-conway2008-1) for his [kis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_kis_operator "Conway kis operator") vertex bisector operation applied to the [rhombille tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombille_tiling "Rhombille tiling"). More specifically it can be called a **3-6 kisrhombille**, to distinguish it from other similar hyperbolic tilings, like [3-7 kisrhombille](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/3-7_kisrhombille "3-7 kisrhombille").

It can be seen as an equilateral [hexagonal tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagonal_tiling "Hexagonal tiling") with each hexagon divided into 12 triangles from the center point. (Alternately it can be seen as a bisected [triangular tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangular_tiling "Triangular tiling") divided into 6 triangles, or as an infinite [arrangement of lines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arrangement_of_lines "Arrangement of lines") in six parallel families.)

It is labeled V4.6.12 because each right triangle face has three types of vertices: one with 4 triangles, one with 6 triangles, and one with 12 triangles.

### Symmetry

The _kisrhombille tiling_ triangles represent the fundamental domains of p6m, [6,3] (*632 [orbifold notation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbifold_notation "Orbifold notation")) [wallpaper group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wallpaper_group "Wallpaper group") symmetry. There are a number of [small index subgroups constructed from [6,3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Template:632_symmetry_table "Template:632 symmetry table") by mirror removal and alternation. [1+,6,3] creates *333 symmetry, shown as red mirror lines. [6,3+] creates 3*3 symmetry. [6,3]+ is the rotational subgroup. The commutator subgroup is [1+,6,3+], which is 333 symmetry. A larger index 6 subgroup constructed as [6,3*], also becomes (*333), shown in blue mirror lines, and which has its own 333 rotational symmetry, index 12.

|Small index subgroups [6,3] (*632)|   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
|[Index](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subgroup_index "Subgroup index")|1|2|   |3|6|   |

## Related polyhedra and tilings

There are eight [uniform tilings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uniform_tiling "Uniform tiling") that can be based from the regular hexagonal tiling (or the dual [triangular tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangular_tiling "Triangular tiling")). Drawing the tiles colored as red on the original faces, yellow at the original vertices, and blue along the original edges, there are 8 forms, 7 which are topologically distinct. (The _truncated triangular tiling_ is topologically identical to the hexagonal tiling.)

|[Uniform hexagonal/triangular tilings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Template:Hexagonal_tiling_table "Template:Hexagonal tiling table")<br><br>- [v](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Template:Hexagonal_tiling_table "Template:Hexagonal tiling table")<br>- [t](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Template_talk:Hexagonal_tiling_table "Template talk:Hexagonal tiling table")<br>- [e](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Hexagonal_tiling_table "Special:EditPage/Template:Hexagonal tiling table")|   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |   |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|

### Symmetry mutations

This tiling can be considered a member of a sequence of uniform patterns with vertex figure (4.6.2p) and [Coxeter-Dynkin diagram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coxeter-Dynkin_diagram "Coxeter-Dynkin diagram") ![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/CDel_node_1.png)![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/CDel_p.png)![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/CDel_node_1.png)![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c3/CDel_3.png)![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/CDel_node_1.png). For _p_ < 6, the members of the sequence are [omnitruncated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omnitruncation_\(geometry\) "Omnitruncation (geometry)") polyhedra ([zonohedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonohedron "Zonohedron")), shown below as spherical tilings. For _p_ > 6, they are tilings of the hyperbolic plane, starting with the [truncated triheptagonal tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_triheptagonal_tiling "Truncated triheptagonal tiling").

# Zonoid

In [convex geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_geometry "Convex geometry"), a **zonoid** is a type of [centrally symmetric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point_symmetry "Point symmetry") [convex body](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_body "Convex body").

## Definitions

The zonoids have several definitions, equivalent up to translations of the resulting shapes:[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b69-1)

- A zonoid is a shape that can be approximated arbitrarily closely (in [Hausdorff distance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hausdorff_distance "Hausdorff distance")) by a [zonotope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonotope "Zonotope"), a [convex polytope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_polytope "Convex polytope") formed from the [Minkowski sum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minkowski_sum "Minkowski sum") of finitely many line segments. In particular, every zonotope is a zonoid.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b69-1) Approximating a zonoid to within Hausdorff distance ε![{\displaystyle \varepsilon }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a30c89172e5b88edbd45d3e2772c7f5e562e5173) requires a number of segments that (for fixed ε![{\displaystyle \varepsilon }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a30c89172e5b88edbd45d3e2772c7f5e562e5173)) is near-linear in the dimension, or linear with some additional assumptions on the zonoid.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-blm-2)
- A zonoid is the [range](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Range_of_a_function "Range of a function") of an atom-free vector-valued [sigma-additive set function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sigma-additive_set_function "Sigma-additive set function"). Here, a function from a family of sets to vectors is sigma-additive when the family is closed under countable disjoint unions, and when the value of the function on a union of sets equals the sum of its values on the sets. It is atom-free when every set whose function value is nonzero has a proper subset whose value remains nonzero. For this definition the resulting shapes contain the origin, but they may be translated arbitrarily as long as they contain the origin.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b69-1) The statement that the shapes described in this way are closed and convex is known as [Lyapunov's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lyapunov%27s_theorem "Lyapunov's theorem").
- A zonoid is the [convex hull](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_hull "Convex hull") of the range of a vector-valued [sigma-additive set function](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sigma-additive_set_function "Sigma-additive set function"). For this definition, being atom-free is not required.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b69-1)
- A zonoid is the [polar body](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polar_set "Polar set") of a central section of the [unit ball](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_ball "Unit ball") of L1([0,1])![{\displaystyle L^{1}([0,1])}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/625838673c09047316c6cd3606a0e6d5ffc8a5c6), the [space of Lebesgue integrable functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lp_space#Lp_spaces_and_Lebesgue_integrals "Lp space") on the unit interval. Here, a central section is the intersection of this ball with a finite-dimensional subspace of L1([0,1])![{\displaystyle L^{1}([0,1])}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/625838673c09047316c6cd3606a0e6d5ffc8a5c6). This definition produces zonoids whose center of symmetry is at the origin.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b69-1)
- A zonoid is a [convex set](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_set "Convex set") whose polar body is a [projection body](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Projection_body "Projection body").[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b69-1)

## Examples

Every two-dimensional centrally-symmetric convex shape is a zonoid.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b71-3) In higher dimensions, the Euclidean [unit ball](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_ball "Unit ball") is a zonoid.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b69-1) A polytope is a zonoid [if and only if](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/If_and_only_if "If and only if") it is a zonotope.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-blm-2) Thus, for instance, the [regular octahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_octahedron "Regular octahedron") is an example of a centrally symmetric convex shape that is not a zonoid.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b69-1)

The [solid of revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid_of_revolution "Solid of revolution") of the positive part of a [sine curve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sine_curve "Sine curve") is a zonoid, obtained as a limit of zonohedra whose generating segments are symmetric to each other with respect to rotations around a common axis.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-cc-4) The [bicones](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bicone "Bicone") provide examples of centrally symmetric solids of revolution that are not zonoids.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b69-1)

## Properties

Zonoids are closed under [affine transformations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Affine_transformation "Affine transformation"),[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-blm-2) under [parallel projection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallel_projection "Parallel projection"),[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-rz-5) and under finite Minkowski sums. Every zonoid that is not a line segment can be decomposed as a Minkowski sum of other zonoids that do not have the same shape as the given zonoid. (This means that they are not translates of [homothetes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homothety "Homothety") of the given zonoid.)[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b69-1)

The zonotopes can be characterized as polytopes having centrally-symmetric pairs of opposite faces, and the _zonoid problem_ is the problem of finding an analogous characterization of zonoids. Ethan Bolker credits the formulation of this problem to a 1916 publication of [Wilhelm Blaschke](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wilhelm_Blaschke "Wilhelm Blaschke").[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid#cite_note-b71-3)


---
# Conway polyhedron notation

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

(Redirected from [Conway kis operator](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Conway_kis_operator&redirect=no "Conway kis operator"))

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Conway_relational_chart.png/500px-Conway_relational_chart.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_relational_chart.png)

This example chart shows how 11 new forms can be derived from the cube using 3 operations. The new polyhedra are shown as maps on the surface of the cube so the topological changes are more apparent. Vertices are marked in all forms with circles.

In [geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry "Geometry") and [topology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topology "Topology"), **Conway polyhedron notation**, invented by [John Horton Conway](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Horton_Conway "John Horton Conway") and promoted by [George W. Hart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Hart "George W. Hart"), is used to describe [polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron "Polyhedron") based on a seed polyhedron modified by various prefix [operations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Operation_\(mathematics\) "Operation (mathematics)").[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-SoT-1)[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-2)

Conway and Hart extended the idea of using operators, like [truncation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncation_\(geometry\) "Truncation (geometry)") as defined by [Kepler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler "Kepler"), to build related polyhedra of the same symmetry. For example, tC represents a [truncated cube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_cube "Truncated cube"), and taC, parsed as _t_(_aC_), is ([topologically](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topology "Topology")) a [truncated cuboctahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_cuboctahedron "Truncated cuboctahedron"). The simplest operator [dual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dual_polyhedron "Dual polyhedron") swaps [vertex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_\(geometry\) "Vertex (geometry)") and [face](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Face_\(geometry\) "Face (geometry)") elements; e.g., a dual [cube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cube "Cube") is an [octahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Octahedron "Octahedron"): _dC_ = _O_. Applied in a series, these operators allow many [higher order polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Higher_order_polyhedra&action=edit&redlink=1 "Higher order polyhedra (page does not exist)") to be generated. Conway defined the operators a (ambo), b ([bevel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bevel "Bevel")), d ([dual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dual_polyhedron "Dual polyhedron")), e (expand), g (gyro), j (join), k (kis), m (meta), o (ortho), s ([snub](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snub_\(geometry\) "Snub (geometry)")), and t ([truncate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncation_\(geometry\) "Truncation (geometry)")), while Hart added r ([reflect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reflection_\(mathematics\) "Reflection (mathematics)")) and p (propellor).[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Hart-3) Later implementations named further operators, sometimes referred to as "extended" operators.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Antiprism-4)[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Levskaya-5) Conway's basic operations are sufficient to generate the [Archimedean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedean_solid "Archimedean solid") and [Catalan solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalan_solid "Catalan solid") from the [Platonic solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platonic_solid "Platonic solid"). Some basic operations can be made as composites of others: for instance, ambo applied twice is the expand operation (_aa_ = _e_), while a truncation after ambo produces bevel (_ta_ = _b_).

Polyhedra can be studied topologically, in terms of how their vertices, edges, and faces connect together, or geometrically, in terms of the placement of those elements in space. Different implementations of these operators may create polyhedra that are geometrically different but topologically equivalent. These topologically equivalent polyhedra can be thought of as one of many [embeddings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graph_embedding "Graph embedding") of a [polyhedral graph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedral_graph "Polyhedral graph") on the sphere. Unless otherwise specified, in this article (and in the literature on Conway operators in general) topology is the primary concern. Polyhedra with [genus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genus_\(mathematics\) "Genus (mathematics)") 0 (i.e. topologically equivalent to a sphere) are often put into [canonical form](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canonical_polyhedron "Canonical polyhedron") to avoid ambiguity.

## Operators

In Conway's notation, operations on polyhedra are applied like functions, from right to left. For example, a [cuboctahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuboctahedron "Cuboctahedron") is an _ambo cube_,[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-hart-6) i.e. ⁠a(C)=aC![{\displaystyle a(C)=aC}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a831b3a2760d0c9246b6bf363f36d93b17a9599c)⁠, and a [truncated cuboctahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_cuboctahedron "Truncated cuboctahedron") is ⁠t(a(C))=t(aC)=taC![{\displaystyle t(a(C))=t(aC)=taC}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/de8faca97e2ef0d1fac59c951b6bca5b4bc11dbe)⁠. Repeated application of an operator can be denoted with an exponent: _j2_ = _o_. In general, Conway operators are not [commutative](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commutative "Commutative").

Individual operators can be visualized in terms of [fundamental domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_domain "Fundamental domain") (or chambers), as below. Each right triangle is a [fundamental domain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fundamental_domain "Fundamental domain"). Each white chamber is a rotated version of the others, and so is each colored chamber. For [achiral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Achiral "Achiral") operators, the colored chambers are a reflection of the white chambers, and all are transitive. In group terms, achiral operators correspond to [dihedral groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dihedral_group "Dihedral group") D_n_ where _n_ is the number of sides of a face, while chiral operators correspond to [cyclic groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyclic_group "Cyclic group") C_n_ lacking the reflective symmetry of the dihedral groups. Achiral and [chiral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chiral "Chiral") operators are also called local symmetry-preserving operations (LSP) and local operations that preserve orientation-preserving symmetries (LOPSP), respectively.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Brinkmann-7)[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-lsp-8)[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-lopsp-9) LSPs should be understood as local operations that preserve symmetry, not operations that preserve local symmetry. Again, these are symmetries in a topological sense, not a geometric sense: the exact angles and edge lengths may differ.

|   |   |   |   |
|---|---|---|---|
Fundamental domains of faces with n![{\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b) sides
|3 (Triangle)|4 (Square)|5 (Pentagon)|6 (Hexagon)|
|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Triangle_chambers.svg/250px-Triangle_chambers.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Triangle_chambers.svg)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Quadrilateral_chambers.svg/250px-Quadrilateral_chambers.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Quadrilateral_chambers.svg)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Pentagon_chambers.svg/250px-Pentagon_chambers.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pentagon_chambers.svg)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Hexagon_chambers.svg/250px-Hexagon_chambers.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hexagon_chambers.svg)|
|The fundamental domains for polyhedron groups. The groups are D3,D4,D5,D6![{\displaystyle D_{3},D_{4},D_{5},D_{6}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7ca5dcbf16e9b5d64b7a5f946ac24a75b49858e2) for achiral polyhedra, and C3,C4,C5,C6![{\displaystyle C_{3},C_{4},C_{5},C_{6}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/05b8f1ddbd3a3bc0c89e944f454a27c1c64f190c) for chiral polyhedra.|   |   |   |

Hart introduced the reflection operator _r_, that gives the mirror image of the polyhedron.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-hart-6) This is not strictly a LOPSP, since it does not preserve orientation: it reverses it, by exchanging white and red chambers. _r_ has no effect on achiral polyhedra aside from orientation, and _rr = S_ returns the original polyhedron. An overline can be used to indicate the other chiral form of an operator: _s_ = _rsr_.

An operation is irreducible if it cannot be expressed as a composition of operators aside from _d_ and _r_. The majority of Conway's original operators are irreducible: the exceptions are _e_, _b_, _o_, and _m_.

### Matrix representation

|   |   |
|---|---|
|x|[abc0d0a′b′c′]=Mx![{\displaystyle {\begin{bmatrix}a&b&c\\0&d&0\\a'&b'&c'\end{bmatrix}}=\mathbf {M} _{x}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8c1b59679b3474ac62ebf51cb7fe0a067a2caa31)|
|xd|[cba0d0c′b′a′]=MxMd![{\displaystyle {\begin{bmatrix}c&b&a\\0&d&0\\c'&b'&a'\end{bmatrix}}=\mathbf {M} _{x}\mathbf {M} _{d}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/662960908e3c96fa3b41730101a5f6c2e0430a49)|
|dx|[a′b′c′0d0abc]=MdMx![{\displaystyle {\begin{bmatrix}a'&b'&c'\\0&d&0\\a&b&c\end{bmatrix}}=\mathbf {M} _{d}\mathbf {M} _{x}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/87b703cca49916a460ffa227d8bf8141b05ff173)|
|dxd|[c′b′a′0d0cba]=MdMxMd![{\displaystyle {\begin{bmatrix}c'&b'&a'\\0&d&0\\c&b&a\end{bmatrix}}=\mathbf {M} _{d}\mathbf {M} _{x}\mathbf {M} _{d}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e2b8eaf11e9c50d49fad6907fa43d69422029c67)|

The relationship between the number of vertices, edges, and faces of the seed and the polyhedron created by the operations listed in this article can be expressed as a matrix Mx![{\displaystyle \mathbf {M} _{x}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cc44dadd8d20d808ce7ce877a7452b8fd171bec2). When _x_ is the operator, v,e,f![{\displaystyle v,e,f}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7e7a363de90560e276c2e52402aceb2743c053b1) are the vertices, edges, and faces of the seed (respectively), and v′,e′,f′![{\displaystyle v',e',f'}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9e0dcca7ce5b8b74fe68231363452c7fe33c5661) are the vertices, edges, and faces of the result, then

Mx[vef]=[v′e′f′]![{\displaystyle \mathbf {M} _{x}{\begin{bmatrix}v\\e\\f\end{bmatrix}}={\begin{bmatrix}v'\\e'\\f'\end{bmatrix}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a26e72f6358a91133501d2a997163082eaa82a89).

The matrix for the composition of two operators is just the product of the matrixes for the two operators. Distinct operators may have the same matrix, for example, _p_ and _l_. The edge count of the result is an integer multiple _d_ of that of the seed: this is called the inflation rate, or the edge factor.[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Brinkmann-7)

The simplest operators, the [identity operator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Identity_operator "Identity operator") _S_ and the [dual operator](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dual_polyhedron "Dual polyhedron") _d_, have simple matrix forms:

MS=[100010001]=I3![{\displaystyle \mathbf {M} _{S}={\begin{bmatrix}1&0&0\\0&1&0\\0&0&1\end{bmatrix}}=\mathbf {I} _{3}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3748296a540e1b4f90ff95863f049f2685af5ed9), Md=[001010100]![{\displaystyle \mathbf {M} _{d}={\begin{bmatrix}0&0&1\\0&1&0\\1&0&0\end{bmatrix}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f027ca30ecce9357173c689f8bba87b1cfff5036)

Two dual operators cancel out; _dd_ = _S_, and the square of Md![{\displaystyle \mathbf {M} _{d}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/04035b455e39f9a0d3e16453fb7eae5223b2c1bd) is the [identity matrix](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Identity_matrix "Identity matrix"). When applied to other operators, the dual operator corresponds to horizontal and vertical reflections of the matrix. Operators can be grouped into groups of four (or fewer if some forms are the same) by identifying the operators _x_, _xd_ (operator of dual), _dx_ (dual of operator), and _dxd_ (conjugate of operator). In this article, only the matrix for _x_ is given, since the others are simple reflections.

### Number of operators

The number of LSPs for each inflation rate is 2,2,4,6,6,20,28,58,82,⋯![{\displaystyle 2,2,4,6,6,20,28,58,82,\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4e0f9e24bb9f5717adf2add6b58dfd2d84d4e3c1) starting with inflation rate 1. However, not all LSPs necessarily produce a polyhedron whose edges and vertices form a [3-connected graph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/K-vertex-connected_graph "K-vertex-connected graph"), and as a consequence of [Steinitz's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steinitz%27s_theorem "Steinitz's theorem") do not necessarily produce a convex polyhedron from a convex seed. The number of 3-connected LSPs for each inflation rate is 2,2,4,6,4,20,20,54,64,⋯![{\displaystyle 2,2,4,6,4,20,20,54,64,\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1971b84d3e0cfe9932e9d774d47ad22f2688ac57).[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-lsp-8) The number of 3-connected LOPSPs (chiral) for each inflation rate is 0,0,0,0,4,0,10,8,38,⋯![{\displaystyle 0,0,0,0,4,0,10,8,38,\cdots }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/76db89b5daf4f6e2d25a31948a5066ce793538df).[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-10)

## Original operations

Strictly, seed (_S_), needle (_n_), and zip (_z_) were not included by Conway, but they are related to original Conway operations by duality so are included here.

From here on, operations are visualized on cube seeds, drawn on the surface of that cube. Blue faces cross edges of the seed, and pink faces lie over vertices of the seed. There is some flexibility in the exact placement of vertices, especially with chiral operators.

Original Conway operators

|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
|1|[100010001]![{\displaystyle {\begin{bmatrix}1&0&0\\0&1&0\\0&0&1\end{bmatrix}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/917dc504a6780a695d578a7b216036af7e49c506)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Conway_C.png/120px-Conway_C.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_C.png)  <br>**Seed**: _S_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6d/Conway_dC.png/120px-Conway_dC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_dC.png)  <br>**Dual**: _d_|   |[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Conway_C.png/120px-Conway_C.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_C.png)  <br>**Seed**: _dd_ = _S_|Dual replaces each face with a vertex, and each vertex with a face.|
|2|[101020010]![{\displaystyle {\begin{bmatrix}1&0&1\\0&2&0\\0&1&0\end{bmatrix}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a4d1d55e81b3442e9a7775fa603c69f82dd3e9b9)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Conway_jC.png/120px-Conway_jC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_jC.png)  <br>**Join**: _j_|   |[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Conway_aC.png/120px-Conway_aC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_aC.png)  <br>**Ambo**: _a_|   |Join creates quadrilateral faces. Ambo creates degree-4 vertices, and is also called [rectification](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rectification_\(geometry\) "Rectification (geometry)"), or the [medial graph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medial_graph "Medial graph") in graph theory.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-11)|
|3|[101030020]![{\displaystyle {\begin{bmatrix}1&0&1\\0&3&0\\0&2&0\end{bmatrix}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9419a43c4ffce9c1e51a5e52bee7c9a445ca72d6)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Conway_kC.png/120px-Conway_kC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_kC.png)  <br>**Kis**: _k_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Conway_kdC.png/120px-Conway_kdC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_kdC.png)  <br>**Needle**: _n_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Conway_dkC.png/120px-Conway_dkC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_dkC.png)  <br>**Zip**: _z_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Conway_tC.png/120px-Conway_tC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_tC.png)  <br>**Truncate**: _t_|Kis raises a pyramid on each face, and is also called akisation, [Kleetope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kleetope "Kleetope"), cumulation,[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-12) accretion, or pyramid-[augmentation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augmentation_\(geometry\) "Augmentation (geometry)"). [Truncate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncation_\(geometry\) "Truncation (geometry)") cuts off the polyhedron at its vertices but leaves a portion of the original edges.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-13) Zip is also called [bitruncation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bitruncation "Bitruncation").|
|4|[111040020]![{\displaystyle {\begin{bmatrix}1&1&1\\0&4&0\\0&2&0\end{bmatrix}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3a0d385c1d46c4a5b8a4fb36c0abdec5f26a3800)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Conway_oC.png/120px-Conway_oC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_oC.png)  <br>**Ortho**: _o_ = _jj_|   |[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Conway_eC.png/120px-Conway_eC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_eC.png)  <br>**Expand**: _e_ = _aa_|   ||
|5|[121050020]![{\displaystyle {\begin{bmatrix}1&2&1\\0&5&0\\0&2&0\end{bmatrix}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/24ec126e243dcd542c4323812da7c5b9bdb71eed)|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Conway_gC.png/120px-Conway_gC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_gC.png)  <br>**Gyro**: _g_|_gd_ = _rgr_|_sd_ = _rsr_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Conway_sC.png/120px-Conway_sC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_sC.png)  <br>**Snub**: _s_|Chiral operators. Contrary to Hart,[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Hart-3) _gd_ is not the same as _g_: it is its chiral pair.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-14)|

## Seeds

Any polyhedron can serve as a seed, as long as the operations can be executed on it. Common seeds have been assigned a letter. The [Platonic solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platonic_solid "Platonic solid") are represented by the first letter of their name ([**T**etrahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrahedron "Tetrahedron"), [**O**ctahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Octahedron "Octahedron"), [**C**ube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cube "Cube"), [**I**cosahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Icosahedron "Icosahedron"), [**D**odecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodecahedron "Dodecahedron")); the [**p**risms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prism_\(geometry\) "Prism (geometry)") (**P**_n_) for _n_-gonal forms; [**a**ntiprisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiprism "Antiprism") (**A**_n_); [c**u**polae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cupola_\(geometry\) "Cupola (geometry)") (**U**_n_); [anticupolae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anticupola "Anticupola") (**V**_n_); and [p**y**ramids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyramid_\(geometry\) "Pyramid (geometry)") (**Y**_n_). Any [**J**ohnson solid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johnson_solid "Johnson solid") can be referenced as **J**_n_, for _n_=1..92.

All of the five Platonic solids can be generated from prismatic generators with zero to two operators:[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-15)

- **[Triangular pyramid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangular_pyramid "Triangular pyramid")**: _Y_3 (A tetrahedron is a special pyramid)
    - _[T](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrahedron "Tetrahedron")_ = _Y_3
    - _[O](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Octahedron "Octahedron")_ = _aT_ (ambo tetrahedron)
    - _[C](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cube "Cube")_ = _jT_ (join tetrahedron)
    - _[I](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_icosahedron "Regular icosahedron")_ = _sT_ (snub tetrahedron)
    - _[D](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_dodecahedron "Regular dodecahedron")_ = _gT_ (gyro tetrahedron)
- **[Triangular antiprism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiprism "Antiprism")**: _A_3 (An octahedron is a special antiprism)
    - _O_ = _A_3
    - _C_ = _dA_3
- **[Square prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prism_\(geometry\) "Prism (geometry)")**: _P_4 (A cube is a special prism)
    - _C_ = _P_4
- **[Pentagonal antiprism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pentagonal_antiprism "Pentagonal antiprism")**: _A_5
    - _I_ = _k_5_A_5 (A special [gyroelongated dipyramid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gyroelongated_dipyramid "Gyroelongated dipyramid"))
    - _D_ = _t_5_dA_5 (A special [truncated trapezohedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_trapezohedron "Truncated trapezohedron"))

The regular Euclidean tilings can also be used as seeds:

- _Q_ = Quadrille = [Square tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_tiling "Square tiling")
- _H_ = Hextille = [Hexagonal tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagonal_tiling "Hexagonal tiling") = _dΔ_
- _Δ_ = Deltille = [Triangular tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangular_tiling "Triangular tiling") = _dH_

## Extended operations

These are operations created after Conway's original set. Note that many more operations exist than have been named; just because an operation is not here does not mean it does not exist (or is not an LSP or LOPSP). In addition, only irreducible operators are included in this list; many others can be created by composing operators together.

## Indexed extended operations

A number of operators can be grouped together by some criteria, or have their behavior modified by an index.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Antiprism-4) These are written as an operator with a subscript: _xn_.

### Augmentation

[Augmentation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Augmentation_\(geometry\) "Augmentation (geometry)") operations retain original edges. They may be applied to any independent subset of faces, or may be converted into a _join_-form by removing the original edges. Conway notation supports an optional index to these operators: 0 for the join-form, or 3 or higher for how many sides affected faces have. For example, _k_4_Y_4=O: taking a square-based pyramid and gluing another pyramid to the square base gives an octahedron.

|   |   |   |   |   |   |   |
|---|---|---|---|---|---|---|
|Augmentation operator|_x_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Conway_kC.png/120px-Conway_kC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_kC.png)<br><br>_k_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Conway_lC.png/120px-Conway_lC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_lC.png)<br><br>_l_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Conway_LC.png/120px-Conway_LC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_LC.png)<br><br>_L_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Conway_KC.png/120px-Conway_KC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_KC.png)<br><br>_K_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Conway_kkC.png/120px-Conway_kkC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_kkC.png)<br><br>_(kk)_|
|Corresponding<br><br>join-form operator|_x_0|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Conway_jC.png/120px-Conway_jC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_jC.png)  <br>_k_0 = _j_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Conway_cC.png/120px-Conway_cC.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_cC.png)  <br>_l_0 = _c_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Conway_L0C.png/120px-Conway_L0C.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_L0C.png)  <br>_L_0|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Conway_K0C.png/120px-Conway_K0C.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_K0C.png)  <br>_K_0 = _jk_|[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Conway_%28kk%290C.png/120px-Conway_%28kk%290C.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_\(kk\)0C.png)  <br>(_kk_)0|
|Augmentation||[Pyramid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyramid_\(geometry\) "Pyramid (geometry)")|[Prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prism_\(geometry\) "Prism (geometry)")|[Antiprism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiprism "Antiprism")|||

The truncate operator _t_ also has an index form _tn_, indicating that only vertices of a certain degree are truncated. It is equivalent to _dknd_.

Some of the extended operators can be created in special cases with _kn_ and _tn_ operators. For example, a [chamfered cube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chamfered_cube "Chamfered cube"), _cC_, can be constructed as _t_4_daC_, as a [rhombic dodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombic_dodecahedron "Rhombic dodecahedron"), _daC_ or _jC_, with its degree-4 vertices truncated. A lofted cube, _lC_ is the same as _t_4_kC_. A quinto-dodecahedron, _qD_ can be constructed as _t_5_daaD_ or _t_5_deD_ or _t_5_oD_, a [deltoidal hexecontahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deltoidal_hexecontahedron "Deltoidal hexecontahedron"), _deD_ or _oD_, with its degree-5 vertices truncated.

### Meta/Bevel

Meta adds vertices at the center and along the edges, while bevel adds faces at the center, seed vertices, and along the edges. The index is how many vertices or faces are added along the edges. Meta (in its non-indexed form) is also called [cantitruncation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cantitruncation "Cantitruncation") or [omnitruncation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omnitruncation "Omnitruncation"). Note that 0 here does not mean the same as for augmentation operations: it means zero vertices (or faces) are added along the edges.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Antiprism-4)

### Medial

Medial is like meta, except it does not add edges from the center to each seed vertex. The index 1 form is identical to Conway's ortho and expand operators: expand is also called [cantellation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cantellation_\(geometry\) "Cantellation (geometry)") and [expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expansion_\(geometry\) "Expansion (geometry)"). Note that _o_ and _e_ have their own indexed forms, described below. Also note that some implementations start indexing at 0 instead of 1.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Antiprism-4)

### Goldberg-Coxeter

The Goldberg-Coxeter (GC) Conway operators are two infinite families of operators that are an extension of the [Goldberg-Coxeter construction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goldberg-Coxeter_construction "Goldberg-Coxeter construction").[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Deza2004-17)[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Deza2015-18) The GC construction can be thought of as taking a triangular section of a triangular lattice, or a square section of a square lattice, and laying that over each face of the polyhedron. This construction can be extended to any face by identifying the chambers of the triangle or square (the "master polygon").[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conway_polyhedron_notation#cite_note-Brinkmann-7) Operators in the triangular family can be used to produce the [Goldberg polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goldberg_polyhedra "Goldberg polyhedra") and [geodesic polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodesic_polyhedra "Geodesic polyhedra"): see [List of geodesic polyhedra and Goldberg polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_geodesic_polyhedra_and_Goldberg_polyhedra "List of geodesic polyhedra and Goldberg polyhedra") for formulas.

The two families are the triangular GC family, _ca,b_ and _ua,b_, and the quadrilateral GC family, _ea,b_ and _oa,b_. Both the GC families are indexed by two integers a≥1![{\displaystyle a\geq 1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c06f20f72b40654833aff35ad637c3bb7c36fe5c) and b≥0![{\displaystyle b\geq 0}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cb90a1049b38d6a352b9bde75bda4cd2f76515dc). They possess many nice qualities:

- The indexes of the families have a relationship with certain [Euclidean domains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclidean_domain "Euclidean domain") over the complex numbers: the [Eisenstein integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eisenstein_integers "Eisenstein integers") for the triangular GC family, and the [Gaussian integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gaussian_integers "Gaussian integers") for the quadrilateral GC family.
- Operators in the _x_ and _dxd_ columns within the same family commute with each other.

The operators are divided into three classes (examples are written in terms of _c_ but apply to all 4 operators):

- Class I: ⁠b=0![{\displaystyle b=0}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/19206e7d4dab695ccb34c502eff0741e98dbdfc2)⁠. Achiral, preserves original edges. Can be written with the zero index suppressed, e.g. _c__a_,0 = _ca_.
- Class II: ⁠a=b![{\displaystyle a=b}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1956b03d1314c7071ac1f45ed7b1e29422dcfcc4)⁠. Also achiral. Can be decomposed as _ca,a_ = _cac_1,1
- Class III: All other operators. These are chiral, and _ca,b_ and _cb,a_ are the chiral pairs of each other.

Of the original Conway operations, the only ones that do not fall into the GC family are _g_ and _s_ (gyro and snub). Meta and bevel (_m_ and _b_) can be expressed in terms of one operator from the triangular family and one from the quadrilateral family.

## Examples

See also: [List of geodesic polyhedra and Goldberg polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_geodesic_polyhedra_and_Goldberg_polyhedra "List of geodesic polyhedra and Goldberg polyhedra")

### Archimedean and Catalan solids

Conway's original set of operators can create all of the [Archimedean solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedean_solids "Archimedean solids") and [Catalan solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalan_solids "Catalan solids"), using the [Platonic solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platonic_solids "Platonic solids") as seeds. (Note that the _r_ operator is not necessary to create both chiral forms.)

- Archimedean
- [![Truncated tetrahedron tT](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Truncated_tetrahedron.png/120px-Truncated_tetrahedron.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Truncated_tetrahedron.png "Truncated tetrahedron tT")
    
    [Truncated tetrahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_tetrahedron "Truncated tetrahedron")  
    _tT_
    
 - [![Cuboctahedron aC = aO = eT](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Cuboctahedron.png/120px-Cuboctahedron.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Cuboctahedron.png "Cuboctahedron aC = aO = eT")
    
    [Cuboctahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuboctahedron "Cuboctahedron")  
    _aC_ = _aO_ = _eT_
    
 - [![Truncated cube tC](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Truncated_hexahedron.png/120px-Truncated_hexahedron.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Truncated_hexahedron.png "Truncated cube tC")

    _tC_

### Composite operators

The [truncated icosahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_icosahedron "Truncated icosahedron"), _tI_, can be used as a seed to create some more visually-pleasing polyhedra, although these are neither [vertex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_transitive "Vertex transitive") nor [face-transitive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Face-transitive "Face-transitive").

- [![tI](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Uniform_polyhedron-53-t12.svg/120px-Uniform_polyhedron-53-t12.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Uniform_polyhedron-53-t12.svg "tI")
    
    _tI_
    
 - [![atI](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Rectified_truncated_icosahedron1.svg/120px-Rectified_truncated_icosahedron1.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Rectified_truncated_icosahedron1.svg "atI")
    
    _atI_
    
 - [![ttI](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Truncated_truncated_icosahedron.png/120px-Truncated_truncated_icosahedron.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Truncated_truncated_icosahedron.png "ttI")
    
    _ttI_
    
 - [![ztI = ttD](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/Conway_polyhedron_Dk6k5tI.png/120px-Conway_polyhedron_Dk6k5tI.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Conway_polyhedron_Dk6k5tI.png "ztI = ttD")
    
    _ztI_ = _ttD_
    
 - [![etI](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Expanded_truncated_icosahedron.png/120px-Expanded_truncated_icosahedron.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Expanded_truncated_icosahedron.png "etI")
    
    _etI_
    
 - [![btI](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Truncated_rectified_truncated_icosahedron.png/120px-Truncated_rectified_truncated_icosahedron.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Truncated_rectified_truncated_icosahedron.png "btI")
    
    _btI_
    
 - [![stI](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Snub_rectified_truncated_icosahedron.png/120px-Snub_rectified_truncated_icosahedron.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Snub_rectified_truncated_icosahedron.png "stI")
    
    _stI_

    _dtI_ = _nI_ = _kD_

    _gtI_
    

### On the plane

Each of the [convex uniform tilings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_convex_uniform_tilings "List of convex uniform tilings") and their duals can be created by applying Conway operators to the [regular tilings](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_tilings "Regular tilings") _Q_, _H_, and _Δ_.

- [![Square tiling Q = dQ = aQ = eQ = jQ = oQ](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/1-uniform_n5.svg/120px-1-uniform_n5.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1-uniform_n5.svg "Square tiling Q = dQ = aQ = eQ = jQ = oQ")
    
    [Square tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_tiling "Square tiling")  
    _Q_ = _dQ_ = _aQ_ = _eQ_  
    = _jQ_ = _oQ_

    [Truncated square tiling](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_square_tiling "Truncated square tiling")  
    _tQ_ = _bQ_
    
 - [![Tetrakis square tiling kQ = mQ](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/1-uniform_2_dual.svg/120px-1-uniform_2_dual.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1-uniform_2_dual.svg "Tetrakis square tiling kQ = mQ")

 - [![Snub square tiling sQ](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/1-uniform_n9.svg/120px-1-uniform_n9.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1-uniform_n9.svg "Snub square tiling sQ")

    _sQ_
    
 - [![Cairo pentagonal tiling gQ](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/1-uniform_9_dual.svg/120px-1-uniform_9_dual.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1-uniform_9_dual.svg "Cairo pentagonal tiling gQ")

- [![Hexagonal tiling H = dΔ = tΔ](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/1-uniform_n1.svg/120px-1-uniform_n1.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:1-uniform_n1.svg "Hexagonal tiling H = dΔ = tΔ")
