# Zonohedron

In [geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry "Geometry"), a **zonohedron** is a [convex polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_polyhedron "Convex polyhedron") that is [centrally symmetric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point_symmetry "Point symmetry"), every face of which is a [polygon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polygon "Polygon") that is centrally symmetric (a [zonogon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonogon "Zonogon")). Any zonohedron may equivalently be described as the [Minkowski sum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minkowski_addition "Minkowski addition") of a set of line segments in three-dimensional space, or as a three-dimensional [projection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Projection_\(mathematics\) "Projection (mathematics)") of a [hypercube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hypercube "Hypercube"). Zonohedra were originally defined and studied by [E. S. Fedorov](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evgraf_Stepanovich_Fyodorov "Evgraf Stepanovich Fyodorov"), a Russian [crystallographer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crystallography "Crystallography"). More generally, in any dimension, the Minkowski sum of line segments forms a [polytope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polytope "Polytope") known as a [zonotope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonotope "Zonotope").

## Tiling space

The original motivation for studying zonohedra is that the [Voronoi diagram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voronoi_diagram "Voronoi diagram") of any [lattice](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lattice_\(group\) "Lattice (group)") forms a [convex uniform honeycomb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_uniform_honeycomb "Convex uniform honeycomb") in which the cells are zonohedra. Any zonohedron formed in this way can [tessellate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Honeycomb_\(geometry\) "Honeycomb (geometry)") 3-dimensional space and is called a [primary parallelohedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallelohedron "Parallelohedron"). Each primary parallelohedron is combinatorially equivalent to one of five types: the [rhombohedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombohedron "Rhombohedron") (including the [cube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cube "Cube")), [hexagonal prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagonal_prism "Hexagonal prism"), [truncated octahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_octahedron "Truncated octahedron"), [rhombic dodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombic_dodecahedron "Rhombic dodecahedron"), and the [rhombo-hexagonal dodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombo-hexagonal_dodecahedron "Rhombo-hexagonal dodecahedron").

## From Minkowski sums

[![Minkowski addition of four line-segments. The left-hand pane displays four sets, which are displayed in a two-by-two array. Each of the sets contains exactly two points, which are displayed in red. In each set, the two points are joined by a pink line-segment, which is the convex hull of the original set. Each set has exactly one point that is indicated with a plus-symbol. In the top row of the two-by-two array, the plus-symbol lies in the interior of the line segment; in the bottom row, the plus-symbol coincides with one of the red-points. This completes the description of the left-hand pane of the diagram. The right-hand pane displays the Minkowski sum of the sets, which is the union of the sums having exactly one point from each summand-set; for the displayed sets, the sixteen sums are distinct points, which are displayed in red: The right-hand red sum-points are the sums of the left-hand red summand-points. The convex hull of the sixteen red-points is shaded in pink. In the pink interior of the right-hand sumset lies exactly one plus-symbol, which is the (unique) sum of the plus-symbols from the right-hand side. The right-hand plus-symbol is indeed the sum of the four plus-symbols from the left-hand sets, precisely two points from the original non-convex summand-sets and two points from the convex hulls of the remaining summand-sets.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Shapley%E2%80%93Folkman_lemma.svg/330px-Shapley%E2%80%93Folkman_lemma.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Shapley%E2%80%93Folkman_lemma.svg)

A zonotope is the Minkowski sum of line segments. The sixteen dark-red points (on the right) form the Minkowski sum of the four non-convex sets (on the left), each of which consists of a pair of red points. Their convex hulls (shaded pink) contain plus-signs (+): The right plus-sign is the sum of the left plus-signs.

Let {v0,v1,…}![{\displaystyle \{v_{0},v_{1},\dots \}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c8d1d0b610811717c004508593cf90d440ea53ea) be a collection of three-dimensional [vectors](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_\(geometric\) "Vector (geometric)"). With each vector vi![{\displaystyle v_{i}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7dffe5726650f6daac54829972a94f38eb8ec127) we may associate a [line segment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Line_segment "Line segment") {xivi∣0≤xi≤1}![{\textstyle \{x_{i}v_{i}\mid 0\leq x_{i}\leq 1\}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3e6561d4d2cec1af510d5469998870c58208818a). The [Minkowski sum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minkowski_addition "Minkowski addition") {∑ixivi∣0≤xi≤1}![{\textstyle \{\textstyle \sum _{i}x_{i}v_{i}\mid 0\leq x_{i}\leq 1\}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a8ad1b707ddfb040436d3c72085a9e21d82c07be) forms a zonohedron, and all zonohedra that contain the origin have this form. The vectors from which the zonohedron is formed are called its **generators**. This characterization allows the definition of zonohedra to be generalized to higher dimensions, giving zonotopes.

Each edge in a zonohedron is parallel to at least one of the generators, and has length equal to the sum of the lengths of the generators to which it is parallel. Therefore, by choosing a set of generators with no parallel pairs of vectors, and by setting all vector lengths equal, we may form an [equilateral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equilateral "Equilateral") version of any combinatorial type of zonohedron.

By choosing sets of vectors with high degrees of symmetry, we can form in this way, zonohedra with at least as much symmetry. For instance, generators equally spaced around the equator of a sphere, together with another pair of generators through the poles of the sphere, form zonohedra in the form of [prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prism_\(geometry\) "Prism (geometry)") over regular 2k![{\displaystyle 2k}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ab358eb7defb4d2b0fc1f9e8a4e2d189fe600eb6)-gons: the [cube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cube "Cube"), [hexagonal prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagonal_prism "Hexagonal prism"), [octagonal prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Octagonal_prism "Octagonal prism"), [decagonal prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decagonal_prism "Decagonal prism"), [dodecagonal prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodecagonal_prism "Dodecagonal prism"), etc. Generators parallel to the edges of an octahedron form a [truncated octahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_octahedron "Truncated octahedron"), and generators parallel to the long diagonals of a cube form a [rhombic dodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombic_dodecahedron "Rhombic dodecahedron").[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonohedron#cite_note-e96-1)

The Minkowski sum of any two zonohedra is another zonohedron, generated by the union of the generators of the two given zonohedra. Thus, the Minkowski sum of a cube and a truncated octahedron forms the [truncated cuboctahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_cuboctahedron "Truncated cuboctahedron"), while the Minkowski sum of the cube and the rhombic dodecahedron forms the [truncated rhombic dodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_rhombic_dodecahedron "Truncated rhombic dodecahedron"). Both of these zonohedra are **simple** (three faces meet at each vertex), as is the [truncated small rhombicuboctahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_small_rhombicuboctahedron "Truncated small rhombicuboctahedron") formed from the Minkowski sum of the cube, truncated octahedron, and rhombic dodecahedron.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonohedron#cite_note-e96-1)

## From arrangements

The [Gauss map](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauss_map "Gauss map") of any convex polyhedron maps each face of the polygon to a point on the unit sphere, and maps each edge of the polygon separating a pair of faces to a [great circle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_circle "Great circle") arc connecting the corresponding two points. In the case of a zonohedron, the edges surrounding each face can be grouped into pairs of parallel edges, and when translated via the Gauss map any such pair becomes a pair of contiguous segments on the same great circle. Thus, the edges of the zonohedron can be grouped into **zones** of parallel edges, which correspond to the segments of a common great circle on the Gauss map, and the 1-[skeleton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skeleton_\(topology\) "Skeleton (topology)") of the zonohedron can be viewed as the [planar dual graph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planar_graph "Planar graph") to an arrangement of great circles on the sphere. Conversely any arrangement of great circles may be formed from the Gauss map of a zonohedron generated by vectors perpendicular to the planes through the circles.

Any simple zonohedron corresponds in this way to a **simplicial arrangement**, one in which each face is a triangle. Simplicial arrangements of great circles correspond via central projection to simplicial [arrangements of lines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arrangement_of_lines "Arrangement of lines") in the [projective plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Projective_plane "Projective plane"). There are three known infinite families of simplicial arrangements, one of which leads to the prisms when converted to zonohedra, and the other two of which correspond to additional infinite families of simple zonohedra. There are also many sporadic examples that do not fit into these three families.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonohedron#cite_note-2)

It follows from the correspondence between zonohedra and arrangements, and from the [Sylvester–Gallai theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sylvester%E2%80%93Gallai_theorem "Sylvester–Gallai theorem") which (in its [projective dual](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Projective_dual "Projective dual") form) proves the existence of crossings of only two lines in any arrangement, that every zonohedron has at least one pair of opposite [parallelogram](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallelogram "Parallelogram") faces. (Squares, rectangles, and rhombuses count for this purpose as special cases of parallelograms.) More strongly, every zonohedron has at least six parallelogram faces, and every zonohedron has a number of parallelogram faces that is linear in its number of generators.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonohedron#cite_note-3)

## Types

Any [prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prism_\(geometry\) "Prism (geometry)") over a regular polygon with an even number of sides forms a zonohedron. These prisms can be formed so that all faces are regular: two opposite faces are equal to the regular polygon from which the prism was formed, and these are connected by a sequence of square faces. Zonohedra of this type are the [cube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cube "Cube"), [hexagonal prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagonal_prism "Hexagonal prism"), [octagonal prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Octagonal_prism "Octagonal prism"), [decagonal prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Decagonal_prism "Decagonal prism"), [dodecagonal prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodecagonal_prism "Dodecagonal prism"), etc.

In addition to this infinite family of regular-faced zonohedra, there are three [Archimedean solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedean_solid "Archimedean solid"), all [omnitruncations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uniform_polyhedron#Convex_forms_and_fundamental_vertex_arrangements "Uniform polyhedron") of the regular forms:

- The [truncated octahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_octahedron "Truncated octahedron"), with 6 square and 8 hexagonal faces. (Omnitruncated tetrahedron)
- The [truncated cuboctahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_cuboctahedron "Truncated cuboctahedron"), with 12 squares, 8 hexagons, and 6 octagons. (Omnitruncated cube)
- The [truncated icosidodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_icosidodecahedron "Truncated icosidodecahedron"), with 30 squares, 20 hexagons and 12 decagons. (Omnitruncated dodecahedron)

In addition, certain [Catalan solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalan_solid "Catalan solid") (duals of Archimedean solids) are again zonohedra:

- [Kepler's rhombic dodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombic_dodecahedron "Rhombic dodecahedron") is the dual of the [cuboctahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cuboctahedron "Cuboctahedron").
- The [rhombic triacontahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombic_triacontahedron "Rhombic triacontahedron") is the dual of the [icosidodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Icosidodecahedron "Icosidodecahedron").

Others with congruent rhombic faces:

- [Bilinski's rhombic dodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bilinski_dodecahedron "Bilinski dodecahedron").
- [Rhombic icosahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombic_icosahedron "Rhombic icosahedron")
- [Rhombohedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombohedron "Rhombohedron")

There are infinitely many zonohedra with rhombic faces that are not all congruent to each other. They include:

- [Rhombic enneacontahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rhombic_enneacontahedron "Rhombic enneacontahedron")

## Dissections

Every zonohedron with n![{\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b) zones can be partitioned into (n3)![{\displaystyle {\tbinom {n}{3}}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bff16d2df20ff95168ed5bfecf62bee1dcefde8d) [parallelepipeds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallelepiped "Parallelepiped"), each having three of the same zones, and with one parallelepiped for each triple of zones.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonohedron#cite_note-4)

The [Dehn invariant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dehn_invariant "Dehn invariant") of any zonohedron is zero. This implies that any two zonohedra with the same [volume](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volume "Volume") can be [dissected](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissection_\(rearrangement\) "Dissection (rearrangement)") into each other. This means that it is possible to cut one of the two zonohedra into polyhedral pieces that can be reassembled into the other.[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonohedron#cite_note-intuitive-5)

## Zonohedrification

Zonohedrification is a process defined by [George W. Hart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Hart "George W. Hart") for creating a zonohedron from another polyhedron.[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonohedron#cite_note-6)[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonohedron#cite_note-7)

First the vertices of any seed polyhedron are considered vectors from the polyhedron center. These vectors create the zonohedron which we call the zonohedrification of the original polyhedron. If the seed polyhedron has [central symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Central_symmetry "Central symmetry"), opposite points define the same direction, so the number of zones in the zonohedron is half the number of vertices of the seed. For any two vertices of the original polyhedron, there are two opposite planes of the zonohedrification which each have two edges parallel to the vertex vectors.

## Zonotopes

The [Minkowski sum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minkowski_sum "Minkowski sum") of [line segments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Line_segments "Line segments") in any dimension forms a type of [polytope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polytope "Polytope") called a [zonotope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonotope "Zonotope"). Equivalently, a zonotope Z![{\displaystyle Z}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1cc6b75e09a8aa3f04d8584b11db534f88fb56bd) generated by vectors v1,...,vk∈Rn![{\displaystyle v_{1},...,v_{k}\in \mathbb {R} ^{n}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a8f283d18f23fd188dd6e15aebffe4ea642d966f) is given by Z={a1v1+⋯+akvk|∀(j)aj∈[0,1]}![{\displaystyle Z=\{a_{1}v_{1}+\cdots +a_{k}v_{k}|\;\forall (j)a_{j}\in [0,1]\}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0021becb167d8cff8c9678bb2db884c6521e8c69). Note that in the special case where k≤n![{\displaystyle k\leq n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/621f658bb51d7caac329d29e9bf435361813777f), the zonotope Z![{\displaystyle Z}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1cc6b75e09a8aa3f04d8584b11db534f88fb56bd) is a (possibly degenerate) [parallelotope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallelohedron#Parallelotope "Parallelohedron").

## Volume

Zonohedra, and _n_-dimensional zonotopes in general, are noteworthy for admitting a simple analytic formula for their volume.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonohedron#cite_note-8)

Let Z(S)![{\displaystyle Z(S)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/73d77d7a09a97760c9483e6270c58c5578a2e04e) be the zonotope Z={a1v1+⋯+akvk|∀(j)aj∈[0,1]}![{\displaystyle Z=\{a_{1}v_{1}+\cdots +a_{k}v_{k}|\;\forall (j)a_{j}\in [0,1]\}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0021becb167d8cff8c9678bb2db884c6521e8c69) generated by a set of vectors S={v1,…,vk∈Rn}![{\displaystyle S=\{v_{1},\dots ,v_{k}\in \mathbb {R} ^{n}\}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5b67a155a3b91461b0efb4a58a849bd431180049). Then the n-dimensional volume of Z(S)![{\displaystyle Z(S)}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/73d77d7a09a97760c9483e6270c58c5578a2e04e) is given by

∑T⊂S:|T|=n|det(Z(T))|![{\displaystyle \sum _{T\subset S\;:\;|T|=n}|\det(Z(T))|}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/399b28bcb047a8892f2262763f297a6c55546882)

The determinant in this formula makes sense because (as noted above) when the set T![{\displaystyle T}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ec7200acd984a1d3a3d7dc455e262fbe54f7f6e0) has cardinality equal to the dimension n![{\displaystyle n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a601995d55609f2d9f5e233e36fbe9ea26011b3b) of the ambient space, the zonotope is a parallelotope.

Note that when k<n![{\displaystyle k<n}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/48ab7000a6f47e3a09a79dcbe31b89272b0c1f1f), this formula simply states that the zonotope has n-volume zero.

## See also

- [Equiprojective polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equiprojective_polyhedra "Equiprojective polyhedra")
- [Zonoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonoid "Zonoid"), the limit shape of a sequence of zonotopes

|Definition|A three-dimensional example of the more general [polytope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polytope "Polytope") in any number of dimensions.|
|Characteristics|number of faces,  <br>topological classification and [Euler characteristic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler_characteristic "Euler characteristic"),  <br>[duality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dual_polyhedron "Dual polyhedron"),  <br>[vertex figures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_figure "Vertex figure"),  <br>[surface area](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_area "Surface area") and [volume](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volume "Volume"),  <br>lines as in [geodesics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodesic "Geodesic") and [diagonals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diagonal "Diagonal"),  <br>[Dehn invariant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dehn_invariant "Dehn invariant"),  <br>[symmetry group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symmetry_group "Symmetry group").|

In [geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometry "Geometry"), a **polyhedron** (pl.: **polyhedra** or **polyhedrons**; from [Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_language "Greek language") [πολύ](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%80%CE%BF%CE%BB%CF%8D%CF%82 "wikt:πολύς") _(poly-)_ 'many' and [ἕδρον](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%95%CE%B4%CF%81%CE%B1 "wikt:ἕδρα") _(-hedron)_ 'base, seat') is a [three-dimensional figure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Three-dimensional_figure "Three-dimensional figure") with flat [polygonal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polygon "Polygon") [faces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Face_\(geometry\) "Face (geometry)"), straight [edges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edge_\(geometry\) "Edge (geometry)") and sharp corners or [vertices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_\(geometry\) "Vertex (geometry)"). The term "polyhedron" may refer either to a [solid figure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solid_figure "Solid figure") or to its boundary [surface](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_\(mathematics\) "Surface (mathematics)"). The terms **solid polyhedron** and **polyhedral surface** are commonly used to distinguish the two concepts.[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-alexandrov-1) Also, the term _polyhedron_ is often used to refer implicitly to the whole [structure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Structure_\(mathematics\) "Structure (mathematics)") formed by a solid polyhedron, its polyhedral surface, its faces, its edges, and its vertices.

There are many definitions of polyhedra, not all of which are equivalent. Under any definition, polyhedra are typically understood to generalize two-dimensional [polygons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polygon "Polygon") and to be the three-dimensional specialization of [polytopes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polytope "Polytope") (a more general concept in any number of [dimensions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dimension "Dimension")). Polyhedra have several general characteristics that include the number of faces, topological classification by [Euler characteristic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler_characteristic "Euler characteristic"), [duality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dual_polyhedron "Dual polyhedron"), [vertex figures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_figure "Vertex figure"), [surface area](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_area "Surface area"), [volume](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volume "Volume"), interior lines, [Dehn invariant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dehn_invariant "Dehn invariant"), and [symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symmetry "Symmetry"). A symmetry of a polyhedron means that the polyhedron's appearance is unchanged by a transformation, such as [rotating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rotation_\(mathematics\) "Rotation (mathematics)") and [reflecting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reflection_\(mathematics\) "Reflection (mathematics)").

**Convex polyhedra** are a well-defined class of polyhedra with several equivalent standard definitions. Every convex polyhedron is the [convex hull](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_hull "Convex hull") of its vertices, and the convex hull of a finite set of points is a polyhedron. Many common families of convex polyhedra, with [cubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cube "Cube") and [pyramids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyramid_\(geometry\) "Pyramid (geometry)") are familiar examples of such.

There exist many miscellaneous families of polyhedra. [Space-filling polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space-filling_polyhedra "Space-filling polyhedra") are those that can be packed together with copies of themselves or with other different types of polyhedra in three-dimensional space. [Flexible polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flexible_polyhedra "Flexible polyhedra") are the polyhedra that can change their overall shape while preserving the shapes of their faces; some have such. [Ideal polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_polyhedron "Ideal polyhedron") is a convex polyhedron defined in three-dimensional [hyperbolic space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperbolic_space "Hyperbolic space"). [Lattice polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lattice_polytope "Lattice polytope") are the convex polyhedra that can be constructed with [integers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer "Integer") coordinates. [Orthogonal polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthogonal_polyhedra "Orthogonal polyhedra") are polyhedra where all edges are orthogonal, parallel to all three axes of [Cartesian coordinate system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system "Cartesian coordinate system"). Copies of polyhedra can share a centre, which is known as [polyhedral compounds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedral_compound "Polyhedral compound"). Polyhedra can be generalized into infinitely many faces called [apeirohedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skew_apeirohedron "Skew apeirohedron"), the underlying space of which is a [complex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number "Complex number") [Hilbert space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert_space "Hilbert space") known as [complex polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_polytope "Complex polytope"), as well as allowing curved faces and edges.

The origin of polyhedra dates back to the ancient era. [Ancient Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Egypt "Ancient Egypt")'s [four-sided Egyptian pyramids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Egyptian_pyramid "Egyptian pyramid") are known for the pyramidal structure, with the study of calculating their volume, specifically the volume of a [frustum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frustum "Frustum"), in [Moscow Mathematical Papyrus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moscow_Mathematical_Papyrus "Moscow Mathematical Papyrus"). In [Ancient Greek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek "Ancient Greek"), the Etruscan dodecahedron was discovered at [Etruscan civilization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Etruscan_civilization "Etruscan civilization") made of [soapstone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soapstone "Soapstone") on [Monte Loffa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Monte_Loffa "Monte Loffa"), and [Platonic solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platonic_solid "Platonic solid") were discovered and studied by [Ancient Greek mathematicians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greek_mathematicians "Ancient Greek mathematicians"), with [Plato](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plato "Plato") describes its association to the [natures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_element "Classical element") for each in his [Timaeus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timaeus_\(dialogue\) "Timaeus (dialogue)"), later soon treatment studied in [Euclid's Elements](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements "Euclid's Elements"). In [Renaissance](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Renaissance "Renaissance"), [toroidal polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toroidal_polyhedra "Toroidal polyhedra") were used for sketching on polyhedral's perspective views, skeletal models, and [nets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_\(geometry\) "Net (geometry)") appearance. [Leonhard Euler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonhard_Euler "Leonhard Euler") worked on [the polyhedral characteristics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler%27s_characteristic "Euler's characteristic") and the solution for the [Seven Bridges of Königsberg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seven_Bridges_of_K%C3%B6nigsberg "Seven Bridges of Königsberg")'s problem, underlying the field of [topology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Topology "Topology"). [Johannes Kepler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johannes_Kepler "Johannes Kepler") discovered two non-convex regular polyhedra, extended by [Louis Poinsot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Poinsot "Louis Poinsot") prepending two more remaining polyhedra, known as [Kepler–Poinsot polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler%E2%80%93Poinsot_polyhedra "Kepler–Poinsot polyhedra"). Many results on polyhedral concepts, like [Hilbert's third problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert%27s_third_problem "Hilbert's third problem"), [Steinitz's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steinitz%27s_theorem "Steinitz's theorem"), and [stellation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellation "Stellation") of Platonic solids. Polyhedra are used in many fields, as well as appearing in biological creatures, nature, and modern [computational geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computational_geometry "Computational geometry").

## Definition

There are several standard definitions of [convex polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_polyhedron "Convex polyhedron"), but they are equivalent, so the notion of convex polyhedron is agreed upon.

In contrast, for the definition of polyhedron in general, no universal agreement exists. As [Branko Grünbaum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Branko_Gr%C3%BCnbaum "Branko Grünbaum") observed,

> The Original Sin in the theory of polyhedra goes back to Euclid, and through Kepler, Poinsot, Cauchy and many others ... at each stage ... the writers failed to define what are the polyhedra.[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-sin-2)

Inequivalent definitions of "polyhedron" have been given in different contexts.[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-lakatos-3) Some of these definitions exclude shapes that have often been counted as polyhedra (such as the [self-crossing polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star_polyhedron "Star polyhedron")) or include shapes that are often not considered as valid polyhedra (such as solids whose boundaries are not [manifolds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manifold "Manifold")). And although several of these definitions explicitly require the number of faces of a polyhedron to be finite, shapes with infinitely many faces such as the [skew apeirohedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skew_apeirohedron "Skew apeirohedron") have also been called polyhedra.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-4) Nevertheless, there is general agreement that a polyhedron is a solid or surface that can be described by its [vertices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_\(geometry\) "Vertex (geometry)") (corner points), [edges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edge_\(geometry\) "Edge (geometry)") (line segments connecting certain pairs of vertices), [faces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Face_\(geometry\) "Face (geometry)") (two-dimensional [polygons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polygon "Polygon")), and that it sometimes can be said to have a particular three-dimensional interior [volume](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volume "Volume"). One can distinguish among these different definitions according to whether they describe the polyhedron as a solid, whether they describe it as a surface, or whether they describe it more abstractly based on its [incidence geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Incidence_geometry "Incidence geometry").[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-5)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Two_orthogonal_polyhedra.svg/250px-Two_orthogonal_polyhedra.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Two_orthogonal_polyhedra.svg)

Two [polycubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polycube "Polycube") (unions of finitely many unit cubes) whose boundary can be covered by finitely many planes but that do not meet some of the more restrictive definitions of polyhedra: The solid on the left has a face that is not a simple polygon, and the solid on the right has a pair of faces that meet in more than one edge.

- A common and somewhat naive definition of a polyhedron is that it is a solid whose boundary can be covered by finitely many planes[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-6)[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-7) or that it is a solid formed as the union of finitely many convex polyhedra.[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-8) Natural refinements of this definition require the solid to be bounded, to have a connected interior, and possibly also to have a connected boundary. The faces of such a polyhedron can be defined as the [connected components](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Connected_space "Connected space") of the parts of the boundary within each of the planes that cover it, and the edges and vertices as the line segments and points where the faces meet. However, the polyhedra defined in this way do not include the self-crossing star polyhedra, whose faces may not form [simple polygons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simple_polygon "Simple polygon"), and some of whose edges may belong to more than two faces.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-9)
- Definitions based on the idea of a bounding surface rather than a solid are also common.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-cromwell-10) For instance, [O'Rourke (1993)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#CITEREFO'Rourke1993) defines a polyhedron as a union of finitely many [convex polygons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_polygon "Convex polygon") (its faces), arranged in space so that the intersection of any two polygons is a shared vertex or edge or the [empty set](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empty_set "Empty set") and so that their union is a [manifold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manifold "Manifold"). If a planar part of such a surface is not itself a convex polygon, O'Rourke requires it to be subdivided into smaller convex polygons, with flat [dihedral angles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dihedral_angle "Dihedral angle") between them.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-11) Somewhat more generally, Grünbaum defines an _acoptic polyhedron_ to be a collection of finitely many simple polygons that form an embedded manifold, with each vertex incident to at least three edges and each two faces intersecting only in shared vertices and edges of each.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-acoptic-12) Cromwell's _[Polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedra_\(book\) "Polyhedra (book)")_ gives a similar definition but without the restriction of at least three edges per vertex. Again, this type of definition does not encompass the self-crossing polyhedra.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-cromwell-10) Similar notions form the basis of topological definitions of polyhedra, as subdivisions of a topological manifold into [topological disks](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Disk_\(mathematics\) "Disk (mathematics)") (the faces) whose pairwise intersections are required to be points (vertices), topological arcs (edges), or the empty set. However, there exist topological polyhedra (even with all faces triangles) that cannot be realized as acoptic polyhedra.[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-13)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Pyramid_abstract_polytope.svg/500px-Pyramid_abstract_polytope.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pyramid_abstract_polytope.svg)

A [square pyramid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_pyramid "Square pyramid") and the associated [abstract polytope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstract_polytope "Abstract polytope"). Here, the elements of a square pyramid can be defined as the partially ordered set.

- One modern approach is based on the theory of [abstract polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abstract_polyhedron "Abstract polyhedron"). These can be defined as [partially ordered sets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partially_ordered_set "Partially ordered set") whose elements are the vertices, edges, and faces of a polyhedron. A vertex or edge element is less than an edge or face element (in this partial order) when the vertex or edge is part of the edge or face. Additionally, one may include a special bottom element of this partial order (representing the empty set) and a top element representing the whole polyhedron. If the sections of the partial order between elements three levels apart (that is, between each face and the bottom element, and between the top element and each vertex) have the same structure as the abstract representation of a polygon, then these partially ordered sets carry exactly the same information as a topological polyhedron.[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_] However, these requirements are often relaxed, to instead require only that sections between elements two levels apart have the same structure as the abstract representation of a line segment.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-bursta-14) (This means that each edge contains two vertices and belongs to two faces, and that each vertex on a face belongs to two edges of that face.) Geometric polyhedra, defined in other ways, can be described abstractly in this way, but it is also possible to use abstract polyhedra as the basis of a definition of geometric polyhedra. A _realization_ of an abstract polyhedron is generally taken to be a mapping from the vertices of the abstract polyhedron to geometric points, such that the points of each face are coplanar. A geometric polyhedron can then be defined as a realization of an abstract polyhedron.[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-grunbaum-same-15) Realizations that omit the requirement of face planarity, that impose additional requirements of symmetry, or that map the vertices to higher dimensional spaces have also been considered.[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-bursta-14) Unlike the solid-based and surface-based definitions, this works perfectly well for star polyhedra. However, without additional restrictions, this definition allows [degenerate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degeneracy_\(mathematics\) "Degeneracy (mathematics)") or unfaithful polyhedra (for instance, by mapping all vertices to a single point) and the question of how to constrain realizations to avoid these degeneracies has not been settled.

In all of these definitions, a polyhedron is typically understood as a three-dimensional example of the more general [polytope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polytope "Polytope") in any number of dimensions. For example, a polygon has a two-dimensional body and no faces, while a [4-polytope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/4-polytope "4-polytope") has a four-dimensional body and an additional set of three-dimensional "cells". However, some of the literature on higher-dimensional geometry uses the term "polyhedron" to mean something else: not a three-dimensional polytope, but a shape that is different from a polytope in some way. For instance, some sources define a convex polyhedron to be the intersection of finitely many [half-spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Half-space_\(geometry\) "Half-space (geometry)"), and a polytope to be a bounded polyhedron.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-polytope-bounded-1-16)[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-polytope-bounded-2-17) The remainder of this article considers only three-dimensional polyhedra.

## General characteristics

### Number of faces

Polyhedra may be classified and are often named according to the number of faces. The naming system is based on Classical Greek, and combines a prefix counting the faces with the suffix "hedron", meaning "base" or "seat" and referring to the faces. For example a [tetrahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrahedron "Tetrahedron") is a polyhedron with four faces, a [pentahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pentahedron "Pentahedron") is a polyhedron with five faces, a [hexahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexahedron "Hexahedron") is a polyhedron with six faces, etc.[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-18) For a complete list of the Greek numeral prefixes see [Numeral prefix § Table of number prefixes in English](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Numeral_prefix#Table_of_number_prefixes_in_English "Numeral prefix"), in the column for Greek cardinal numbers. The names of tetrahedra, hexahedra, octahedra (eight-sided polyhedra), dodecahedra (twelve-sided polyhedra), and icosahedra (twenty-sided polyhedra) are sometimes used without additional qualification to refer to the [Platonic solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platonic_solid "Platonic solid"), and sometimes used to refer more generally to polyhedra with the given number of sides without any assumption of symmetry.[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-19)

### Topological classification

Main articles: [Toroidal polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toroidal_polyhedron "Toroidal polyhedron") and [Euler characteristic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler_characteristic "Euler characteristic")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Tetrahemihexahedron_rotation.gif/250px-Tetrahemihexahedron_rotation.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tetrahemihexahedron_rotation.gif)

The [tetrahemihexahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrahemihexahedron "Tetrahemihexahedron"), a non-orientable self-intersecting polyhedron with four triangular faces (red) and three square faces (yellow). As with a [Möbius strip](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M%C3%B6bius_strip "Möbius strip") or [Klein bottle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Klein_bottle "Klein bottle"), a continuous path along the surface of this polyhedron can reach the point on the opposite side of the surface from its starting point, making it impossible to separate the surface into an inside and an outside. (Topologically, this polyhedron is a [real projective plane](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_projective_plane "Real projective plane").)

Some polyhedra have two distinct sides to their surface. For example, the inside and outside of a convex polyhedron paper model can each be given a different colour (although the inside colour will be hidden from view). These polyhedra are [orientable](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orientability "Orientability"). The same is true for non-convex polyhedra without self-crossings. Some non-convex self-crossing polyhedra can be coloured in the same way but have regions turned "inside out" so that both colours appear on the outside in different places; these are still considered to be orientable. However, for some other self-crossing polyhedra with simple-polygon faces, such as the [tetrahemihexahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrahemihexahedron "Tetrahemihexahedron"), it is not possible to colour the two sides of each face with two different colours so that adjacent faces have consistent colours. In this case the polyhedron is said to be non-orientable. For polyhedra with self-crossing faces, it may not be clear what it means for adjacent faces to be consistently coloured, but for these polyhedra it is still possible to determine whether it is orientable or non-orientable by considering a topological [cell complex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cell_complex "Cell complex") with the same incidences between its vertices, edges, and faces.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-ringel-20)

A more subtle distinction between polyhedron surfaces is given by their [Euler characteristic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euler_characteristic "Euler characteristic"), which combines the numbers of vertices V![{\displaystyle V}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/af0f6064540e84211d0ffe4dac72098adfa52845), edges E![{\displaystyle E}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4232c9de2ee3eec0a9c0a19b15ab92daa6223f9b), and faces F![{\displaystyle F}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/545fd099af8541605f7ee55f08225526be88ce57) of a polyhedron into a single number χ![{\displaystyle \chi }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/656111758322ace96d80a9371771aa6d3de25437) defined by the formula

χ=V−E+F. ![{\displaystyle \chi =V-E+F.\ }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e322cd3b5333d260c4ace1e89991893921cc370) 

The same formula is also used for the Euler characteristic of other kinds of topological surfaces. It is an invariant of the surface, meaning that when a single surface is subdivided into vertices, edges, and faces in more than one way, the Euler characteristic will be the same for these subdivisions. For a convex polyhedron, or more generally any simply connected polyhedron with the surface of a topological sphere, it always equals 2. For more complicated shapes, the Euler characteristic relates to the number of [toroidal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toroid "Toroid") holes, handles or [cross-caps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cross-cap "Cross-cap") in the surface and will be less than 2.[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-21) All polyhedra with odd-numbered Euler characteristics are non-orientable. A given figure with even Euler characteristic may or may not be orientable. For example, the one-holed [toroid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toroid "Toroid") and the [Klein bottle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Klein_bottle "Klein bottle") both have χ=0![{\displaystyle \chi =0}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fa2a52c29ba6859766c02e88299b3114d010e3b4), with the first being orientable and the other not.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-ringel-20)

For many (but not all) ways of defining polyhedra, the surface of the polyhedron is required to be a [manifold](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Manifold "Manifold"). This means that every edge is part of the boundary of exactly two faces (disallowing shapes like the union of two cubes that meet only along a shared edge) and that every vertex is incident to a single alternating cycle of edges and faces (disallowing shapes like the union of two cubes sharing only a single vertex). For polyhedra defined in these ways, the [classification of manifolds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classification_of_manifolds "Classification of manifolds") implies that the topological type of the surface is completely determined by the combination of its Euler characteristic and orientability. For example, every polyhedron whose surface is an orientable manifold and whose Euler characteristic is 2 must be a topological sphere.[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-ringel-20)

A [toroidal polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toroidal_polyhedron "Toroidal polyhedron") is a polyhedron whose Euler characteristic is less than or equal to 0, or equivalently whose [genus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genus_\(mathematics\) "Genus (mathematics)") is 1 or greater. Topologically, the surfaces of such polyhedra are [torus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Torus "Torus") surfaces having one or more holes through the middle.[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-22) A notable example is the [Szilassi polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Szilassi_polyhedron "Szilassi polyhedron"), which geometrically realizes the [Heawood map](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heawood_map "Heawood map"). A polyhedron with the symmetries of a regular polyhedron and with genus more than one is a [Leonardo polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonardo_polyhedron "Leonardo polyhedron").[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-23)

### Duality

Main article: [Dual polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dual_polyhedron "Dual polyhedron")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Dual_Cube-Octahedron.svg/250px-Dual_Cube-Octahedron.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Dual_Cube-Octahedron.svg)

The octahedron is dual to the cube

For every convex polyhedron, there exists a dual polyhedron having

- faces in place of the original's vertices and vice versa, and
- the same number of edges.

The dual of a convex polyhedron can be obtained by the process of [polar reciprocation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dual_polyhedron#Polar_reciprocation "Dual polyhedron").[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-24) Dual polyhedra exist in pairs, and the dual of a dual is just the original polyhedron again. Some polyhedra are self-dual, meaning that the dual of the polyhedron is congruent to the original polyhedron.[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-25)

Abstract polyhedra also have duals, obtained by reversing the [partial order](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Partial_order "Partial order") defining the polyhedron to obtain its [dual or opposite order](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duality_\(order_theory\) "Duality (order theory)").[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-grunbaum-same-15) These have the same Euler characteristic and orientability as the initial polyhedron. However, this form of duality does not describe the shape of a dual polyhedron, but only its combinatorial structure. For some definitions of non-convex geometric polyhedra, there exist polyhedra whose abstract duals cannot be realized as geometric polyhedra under the same definition.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-acoptic-12)

### Vertex figures

Main article: [Vertex figure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_figure "Vertex figure")

For every vertex one can define a [vertex figure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_figure "Vertex figure"), which describes the local structure of the polyhedron around the vertex. Precise definitions vary, but a vertex figure can be thought of as the polygon exposed where a slice through the polyhedron cuts off a vertex.[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-cromwell-10) For the [Platonic solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platonic_solid "Platonic solid") and other highly-symmetric polyhedra, this slice may be chosen to pass through the midpoints of each edge incident to the vertex,[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-26) but other polyhedra may not have a plane through these points. For convex polyhedra, and more generally for polyhedra whose vertices are in [convex position](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_position "Convex position"), this slice can be chosen as any plane separating the vertex from the other vertices.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-27) When the polyhedron has a center of symmetry, it is standard to choose this plane to be perpendicular to the line through the given vertex and the center;[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-28) with this choice, the shape of the vertex figure is determined up to scaling. When the vertices of a polyhedron are not in convex position, there will not always be a plane separating each vertex from the rest. In this case, it is common instead to slice the polyhedron by a small sphere centered at the vertex.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-29) Again, this produces a shape for the vertex figure that is invariant up to scaling. All of these choices lead to vertex figures with the same combinatorial structure, for the polyhedra to which they can be applied, but they may give them different geometric shapes.

### Surface area and lines inside polyhedra

The [surface area](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_area "Surface area") of a polyhedron is the sum of the areas of its faces, for definitions of polyhedra for which the area of a face is well-defined. The [geodesic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geodesic "Geodesic") distance between any two points on the surface of a polyhedron measures the length of the shortest curve that connects the two points, remaining within the surface. By [Alexandrov's uniqueness theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alexandrov%27s_uniqueness_theorem "Alexandrov's uniqueness theorem"), every convex polyhedron is uniquely determined by the [metric space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metric_space "Metric space") of geodesic distances on its surface. However, non-convex polyhedra can have the same surface distances as each other, or the same as certain convex polyhedra.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-30)

When a line segment connect two vertices that are not in the same face, it forms a [diagonal line](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Diagonal "Diagonal") of the polyhedron.[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-pb-31) Not all polyhedra have diagonal lines, as in the family of [pyramids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyramid_\(geometry\) "Pyramid (geometry)")[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-32). The [Schönhardt polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sch%C3%B6nhardt_polyhedron "Schönhardt polyhedron") has three diagonal lines, all of which lie entirely outside of it, and the [Császár polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cs%C3%A1sz%C3%A1r_polyhedron "Császár polyhedron") has no diagonal lines (rather, every pair of vertices is connected by an edge).[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-bagemihl-33)

### Volume

Polyhedral solids have an associated quantity called [volume](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volume "Volume") that measures how much space they occupy. Simple families of solids may have simple formulas for their volumes; for example, the volumes of pyramids, prisms, and [parallelepipeds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallelepiped "Parallelepiped") can easily be expressed in terms of their edge lengths or other coordinates. (See [Volume § Volume formulas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Volume#Formulas "Volume") for a list that includes many of these formulas.)

Volumes of more complicated polyhedra may not have simple formulas. The volumes of such polyhedra may be computed by subdividing the polyhedron into smaller pieces (for example, by [triangulation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point-set_triangulation "Point-set triangulation")). For example, the [volume of a Platonic solid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platonic_solid#Radii,_area,_and_volume "Platonic solid") can be computed by dividing it into congruent [pyramids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyramid_\(geometry\) "Pyramid (geometry)"), with each pyramid having a face of the polyhedron as its base and the centre of the polyhedron as its apex.

In general, it can be derived from the [divergence theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Divergence_theorem "Divergence theorem") that the volume of a polyhedral solid is given by13|∑F(QF⋅NF)area⁡(F)|,![{\displaystyle {\frac {1}{3}}\left|\sum _{F}(Q_{F}\cdot N_{F})\operatorname {area} (F)\right|,}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/57163b6827e62c51d639c81c07e9ba7230bf8f22)where the sum is over faces F![{\displaystyle F}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/545fd099af8541605f7ee55f08225526be88ce57) of the polyhedron, QF![{\displaystyle Q_{F}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8e6b647a43c373e63abe85a18f2d97579f75a654) is an arbitrary point on face F![{\displaystyle F}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/545fd099af8541605f7ee55f08225526be88ce57), NF![{\displaystyle N_{F}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/014afc9add152bccbb15c18cdb3c2bf1e1b89aa1) is the [unit vector](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_vector "Unit vector") perpendicular to F![{\displaystyle F}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/545fd099af8541605f7ee55f08225526be88ce57) pointing outside the solid, and the multiplication dot is the [dot product](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dot_product "Dot product").[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-34) In higher dimensions, volume computation may be challenging, in part because of the difficulty of listing the faces of a convex polyhedron specified only by its vertices, and there exist specialized [algorithms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algorithm "Algorithm") to determine the volume in these cases.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-35)

### Dehn invariant

Main article: [Dehn invariant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dehn_invariant "Dehn invariant")

In two dimensions, the [Bolyai–Gerwien theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bolyai%E2%80%93Gerwien_theorem "Bolyai–Gerwien theorem") asserts that any polygon may be transformed into any other polygon of the same area by [cutting it up into finitely many polygonal pieces and rearranging them](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dissection_problem "Dissection problem"). The analogous question for polyhedra was the subject of [Hilbert's third problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert%27s_third_problem "Hilbert's third problem"). [Max Dehn](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Max_Dehn "Max Dehn") solved this problem by showing that, unlike in the 2-D case, there exist polyhedra of the same volume that cannot be cut into smaller polyhedra and reassembled into each other. To prove this Dehn discovered another value associated with a polyhedron, the [Dehn invariant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dehn_invariant "Dehn invariant"), such that two polyhedra can only be dissected into each other when they have the same volume and the same Dehn invariant. It was later proven by Sydler that this is the only obstacle to dissection: every two Euclidean polyhedra with the same volumes and Dehn invariants can be cut up and reassembled into each other.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-36) The Dehn invariant is not a number, but a [vector](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vector_\(mathematics\) "Vector (mathematics)") in an infinite-dimensional vector space, determined from the lengths and [dihedral angles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dihedral_angle "Dihedral angle") of a polyhedron's edges.[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-37)

Another of Hilbert's problems, [Hilbert's eighteenth problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert%27s_18th_problem "Hilbert's 18th problem"), concerns (among other things) polyhedra that [tile space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Honeycomb_\(geometry\) "Honeycomb (geometry)"). Every such polyhedron must have Dehn invariant zero.[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-38) The Dehn invariant has also been connected to [flexible polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flexible_polyhedron "Flexible polyhedron") by the strong bellows theorem, which states that the Dehn invariant of any flexible polyhedron remains invariant as it flexes.[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-39)

### Net

Main article: [Net (polyhedron)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_\(polyhedron\) "Net (polyhedron)")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Dodecahedron_flat.svg/250px-Dodecahedron_flat.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Dodecahedron_flat.svg)

A net of a [regular dodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_dodecahedron "Regular dodecahedron")

The faces of some polyhedra can be unfolded into an arrangement of non-overlapping [edge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edge_\(geometry\) "Edge (geometry)")-joined polygons in the plane. Such an arrangement is known as a [net of the polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Net_\(polyhedron\) "Net (polyhedron)"). Nets can be used to construct [polyhedron models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron_model "Polyhedron model") from paper or other flexible materials.[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-weinnger-40)

## Symmetries

[](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Revolu%C3%A7%C3%A3o_de_poliedros_03.webm "Play video")[](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Revolu%C3%A7%C3%A3o_de_poliedros_03.webm)

Some polyhedra rotating around a symmetrical axis (at [Matemateca IME-USP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matemateca "Matemateca"))

Many of the most studied polyhedra are highly [symmetrical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symmetry "Symmetry"). Their appearance is unchanged by some reflection by plane or rotation around the [axes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axis_of_symmetry "Axis of symmetry") passing through two opposite vertices, edges, or faces in space. Each symmetry may change the location of a given element, but the set of all vertices (likewise faces and edges) is unchanged. The collection of symmetries of a polyhedron is called its [symmetry group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symmetry_group "Symmetry group").[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-akl-41)

### By elements of polyhedron

All the elements (vertex, face, and edge) that can be superimposed on each other by symmetries are said to form a [symmetry orbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symmetry_orbit#Orbits_and_stabilizers "Symmetry orbit"). If these elements lie in the same orbit, the figure may be transitive on the orbit. Individually, they are [isohedral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isohedral "Isohedral") (or face-transitive, meaning the symmetry transformations involve the polyhedra's faces in orbit),[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-mclean-42)[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-43) [isotoxal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isotoxal "Isotoxal") (or edge-transitive, which involves the edge's polyhedra),[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-grunbaum-1997-44) and [isogonal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isogonal_figure "Isogonal figure") (or vertex-transitive, which involves the polyhedra's vertices). For example, the [cube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cube "Cube") in which all the faces are in one orbit and involving the rotation and reflections in the orbit remains unchanged in its appearance; hence, the cube is face-transitive. The cube also has the other two such symmetries.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-senechal-45)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Hexahedron.svg/250px-Hexahedron.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hexahedron.svg)

The [cube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cube "Cube") is a [regular polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_polyhedron "Regular polyhedron"), because its faces, edges, and vertices are transitive to another, and the appearance is unchanged.

When three such symmetries belong to a polyhedron, it is known as a [regular polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_polyhedron "Regular polyhedron").[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-senechal-45) There are nine regular polyhedra: five [Platonic solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platonic_solid "Platonic solid") (cube, [octahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_octahedron "Regular octahedron"), [icosahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_icosahedron "Regular icosahedron"), [tetrahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_tetrahedron "Regular tetrahedron"), and [dodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_dodecahedron "Regular dodecahedron")—all of which have regular polygonal faces) and four [Kepler–Poinsot polyhedrons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler%E2%80%93Poinsot_polyhedron "Kepler–Poinsot polyhedron"). Nevertheless, some polyhedrons may not possess one or two of those symmetries:

- A polyhedron that is vertex-transitive and edge-transitive is said to be [quasiregular](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quasiregular_polyhedron "Quasiregular polyhedron"); they have regular faces, and their duals are face-transitive and edge-transitive.
- A vertex- but not edge-transitive polyhedron with regular polygonal faces is said to be [semiregular](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semiregular_polyhedron "Semiregular polyhedron").[[b]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-46) This includes the [prisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prism_\(geometry\) "Prism (geometry)") and [antiprisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiprism "Antiprism"). The dual of a semiregular polyhedron is face-transitive, and every vertex is regular.
- A vertex-transitive polyhedron with regular polygonal faces is said to be [uniform](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uniform_polyhedron "Uniform polyhedron"). This class includes the regular, quasi-regular, and semi-regular polyhedra. The definition may be applied to both convex and star polyhedra. The dual of a uniform polyhedron is face-transitive and has regular vertices but is not necessarily vertex-transitive. The uniform polyhedra and their duals are traditionally classified according to their degree of symmetry, and whether they are convex or not.
- A face- and vertex-transitive (but not necessarily edge-transitive) polyhedron is said to be [noble](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noble_polyhedron "Noble polyhedron"). The regular polyhedra are also noble; they are the only noble uniform polyhedra. The duals of noble polyhedra are themselves noble.

Some polyhedra have no [reflection symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reflection_symmetry "Reflection symmetry"), so that they have two enantiomorph forms, which are reflections of each other. Examples include the [snub cuboctahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snub_cuboctahedron "Snub cuboctahedron") and [snub icosidodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snub_icosidodecahedron "Snub icosidodecahedron"). In this case the polyhedron is said to be [chiral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chirality_\(mathematics\) "Chirality (mathematics)").

### By point group in three dimensions

Main article: [Point groups in three dimensions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point_groups_in_three_dimensions "Point groups in three dimensions")

The [point group of polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point_groups_in_three_dimensions "Point groups in three dimensions") means a [mathematical group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_group "Mathematical group") endowed with its [symmetry operations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symmetry_operation "Symmetry operation") so that the appearance of polyhedra remains preserved while transforming in three-dimensional space. The indicated transformation here includes the rotation around the axes, reflection through the plane, inversion through a center point, and a combination of these three.[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-powell-47)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Symmetries_of_the_tetrahedron.svg/330px-Symmetries_of_the_tetrahedron.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Symmetries_of_the_tetrahedron.svg)

The regular tetrahedron has full tetrahedral symmetry: three-fold rotation around axis passing both vertex and triangular face, and two-fold rotation around axis through two edges, as well as the reflection plane through two faces and one edge

The [polyhedral group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedral_group "Polyhedral group") is the symmetry group originally derived from the three Platonic solids: tetrahedron, octahedron, and icosahedron. These three have point groups respectively known as [tetrahedral symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrahedral_symmetry "Tetrahedral symmetry"), [octahedral symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Octahedral_symmetry "Octahedral symmetry"), and [icosahedral symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Icosahedral_symmetry "Icosahedral symmetry"). Each of these focuses on the rotation group of polyhedra, known as the _chiral polyhedral group_, whereas the additional reflection symmetry is known as the _full polyhedral group_. One point group, [pyritohedral symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyritohedral_symmetry "Pyritohedral symmetry"), includes the rotation of tetrahedral symmetry and additionally has three planes of reflection symmetry and some [rotoreflections](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rotoreflection "Rotoreflection"). Overall, the mentioned polyhedral groups are summarized in the following bullets:[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-fsz-48)

- chiral tetrahedral symmetry T![{\displaystyle \mathrm {T} }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/06cc73e47284b51d2ab60d333176c2366a333e7d), the rotation group for a regular tetrahedron and has the order of twelve.
- full tetrahedral symmetry Td![{\displaystyle \mathrm {T} _{\mathrm {d} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d1c79d7b897d16f2a258ca3baea4724ba1486f3a), the symmetry group for a regular tetrahedron and has the order of twenty-four.
- pyritohedral symmetry Th![{\displaystyle \mathrm {T} _{\mathrm {h} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d4341172931a51303ace861a83995e9231f6c253), the symmetry of a [pyritohedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyritohedron "Pyritohedron") and has the order of twenty-four.
- chiral octahedral symmetry O![{\displaystyle \mathrm {O} }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f3d6d4173d32feed308e80dbaf00e1274f40702d), the rotation group of both cube and regular octahedron and has the order twenty-four.
- full octahedral symmetry Oh![{\displaystyle \mathrm {O} _{\mathrm {h} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d3c0a7669167dbe00f64525845a9c7bff1b39ef9), the symmetry group of both cube and regular octahedron and has order forty-eight.
- chiral icosahedral symmetry I![{\displaystyle \mathrm {I} }](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/fe7a69180f25bbb4c73e091f97c7c5f9941ed17b), the rotation group of both regular icosahedron and regular dodecahedron and has the order of sixty.
- full icosahedral symmetry Ih![{\displaystyle \mathrm {I} _{\mathrm {h} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a9fdfce71bda597451d015e732f835e6fc14b348), the symmetry group of both regular icosahedron and regular dodecahedron and has the order of a hundred-twenty.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Square_pyramid.png/250px-Square_pyramid.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Square_pyramid.png)

The [square pyramid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Square_pyramid "Square pyramid") has pyramidal symmetry C4v![{\displaystyle C_{4\mathrm {v} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/222fdec1d220cb23bdee7f21b3b1fd0f2eb7d930). It shows the appearance is invariant by rotating every quarter of a full turn around its axis and possesses [mirror symmetric](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mirror_symmetric "Mirror symmetric") relative to any perpendicular plane passing through its base's bisector

Point groups in three dimensions may also allow the preservation of polyhedra's appearance by the circulation around an axis. There are three various of these point groups:

- [pyramidal symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyramidal_symmetry "Pyramidal symmetry") Cnv![{\displaystyle C_{n\mathrm {v} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/07e766b0b83dc15a2934d3fbdc0b107d173675c5), allowing rotate the axis passing through the [apex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apex_\(geometry\) "Apex (geometry)") and its [base](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Base_\(geometry\) "Base (geometry)"), as well as reflection relative to perpendicular planes passing through the bisector of a base. This point group symmetry can be found in pyramids,[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlusserSukZitofa2017[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidjwKLDQAAQBAJpgPA126_126]-49) [cupolas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cupola_\(geometry\) "Cupola (geometry)"), and [rotundas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rotunda_\(geometry\) "Rotunda (geometry)").
- [prismatic symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prismatic_symmetry "Prismatic symmetry") Dnh![{\displaystyle D_{n\mathrm {h} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9864821fb3fc727fa93646c57c7c9148de19ad2c), similar to the pyramidal symmetry, but with additional transformation by reflecting it across a horizontal plane.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlusserSukZitofa2017[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidjwKLDQAAQBAJpgPA126_126]-49) This may be achieved from the family of prisms and its dual [bipyramids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bipyramid "Bipyramid").[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlusserSukZitofa2017[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidjwKLDQAAQBAJpgPA126_126]-49)
- [antiprismatic symmetry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiprismatic_symmetry "Antiprismatic symmetry") Dnv![{\displaystyle D_{n\mathrm {v} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ec25820923d1570a4099c6a360e2981fd73a56df), which preserves the symmetry by rotating its half bottom and reflection across the horizontal plane.[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlusserSukZitofa2017[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidjwKLDQAAQBAJpgPA126_126]-49) Examples can be found in antiprisms.

A point group Cnh![{\displaystyle C_{n\mathrm {h} }}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/869785d1c6fd2a572e3e71011ce76eb5854e8ee9) consists of rotating around the axis of symmetry and reflection on the horizontal plane. In the case of n=1![{\displaystyle n=1}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d9ec7e1edc2e6d98f5aec2a39ae5f1c99d1e1425), the symmetry group only preserves the symmetry by a full rotation solely, ordinarily denoting Cs![{\displaystyle C_{s}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3b6d160c0ac08478db6e852c204a78cd9bc2e14d).[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-herbert-50) Polyhedra may have rotation only to preserve the symmetry, and the symmetry group may be considered as the [cyclic group](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cyclic_group "Cyclic group") Cn![{\displaystyle C_{n}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0301812adb392070d834ca2df4ed97f1cf132f33).[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlusserSukZitofa2017[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidjwKLDQAAQBAJpgPA125_125]-51) Polyhedra with the rotoreflection and the rotation by the cyclic group is the point group Sn![{\displaystyle S_{n}}](https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9f049ac28d4ac8097b625f9d71c1f22b2ebd1bc4).[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHergertGeilhufe2018[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6mvpCgAAQBAJpgPA57_57]-52)

## Convex polyhedra

|   |   |
|---|---|
|![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Split-arrows.svg/60px-Split-arrows.svg.png)|It has been suggested that this section be [**split**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Splitting "Wikipedia:Splitting") out into another article titled _[Convex polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_polyhedron "Convex polyhedron")_. ([**Discuss**](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Talk:Polyhedron#Split_convex_polyhedra "Talk:Polyhedron")) _(November 2025)_|

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Hexagonal_pyramid.png/250px-Hexagonal_pyramid.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hexagonal_pyramid.png)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Afgeknotte_driezijdige_piramide.svg/250px-Afgeknotte_driezijdige_piramide.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Afgeknotte_driezijdige_piramide.svg)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Triakisicosahedron.jpg/250px-Triakisicosahedron.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Triakisicosahedron.jpg)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Triaugmented_triangular_prism_%28symmetric_view%29.svg/250px-Triaugmented_triangular_prism_%28symmetric_view%29.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Triaugmented_triangular_prism_\(symmetric_view\).svg)

Top left to bottom right: [hexagonal pyramid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hexagonal_pyramid "Hexagonal pyramid") (a [prismatoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prismatoid "Prismatoid")), [truncated tetrahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Truncated_tetrahedron "Truncated tetrahedron") (an [Archimedean solid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedean_solid "Archimedean solid")), [triakis icosahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triakis_icosahedron "Triakis icosahedron") (a [Catalan solid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalan_solid "Catalan solid")), and [triaugmented triangular prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triaugmented_triangular_prism "Triaugmented triangular prism") (a [Johnson solid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johnson_solid "Johnson solid") and [deltahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deltahedron "Deltahedron")). All of these classes are convex polyhedra.

As [mentioned above](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#Definition), the convex polyhedra are well-defined, with several equivalent standard definitions. They are often defined as bounded intersections of finitely many [half-spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Half-space_\(geometry\) "Half-space (geometry)"),[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-polytope-bounded-1-16)[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-polytope-bounded-2-17) or as the [convex hull](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_hull "Convex hull") of finitely many points,[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-bk-53) restricted in either case to intersections or hulls that have nonzero volume.

The classes of convex polyhedra include the following:

- the family of [prismatoid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prismatoid "Prismatoid"), polyhedra whose vertices lie on two parallel planes and whose faces are likely to be trapezoids and triangles.[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-prismatoid-54) Examples of prismatoids are [pyramids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pyramid_\(geometry\) "Pyramid (geometry)"), [wedges](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wedge_\(geometry\) "Wedge (geometry)"), [parallelipipeds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallelipiped "Parallelipiped"), [prisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prism_\(geometry\) "Prism (geometry)"), [antiprisms](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiprism "Antiprism"), [cupolas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cupola_\(geometry\) "Cupola (geometry)"), and [frustums](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frustum "Frustum").
- [Platonic solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Platonic_solid "Platonic solid") are the five ancient polyhedra—[tetrahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_tetrahedron "Regular tetrahedron"), [octahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_octahedron "Regular octahedron"), [icosahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_icosahedron "Regular icosahedron"), [cube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cube "Cube"), and [dodecahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Regular_dodecahedron "Regular dodecahedron")—described by [Plato](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plato "Plato") in the [_Timaeus_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timaeus_\(dialogue\) "Timaeus (dialogue)").[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-FOOTNOTECromwell199751&ndash;52-55) [Archimedean solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedean_solids "Archimedean solids") are the class of thirteen polyhedra whose faces are all regular polygons and whose vertices are symmetric to each other;[[c]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-57) their dual polyhedra are the [Catalan solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catalan_solid "Catalan solid").[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-diudea-58)
