- [Johnson solids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johnson_solid "Johnson solid") are a class of 92 convex polyhedra whose faces are all regular polygons, excluding the [uniform polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Uniform_polyhedra "Uniform polyhedra"), namely the Platonic and Archimedean solids, as well as the infinite families of prisms and antiprisms.[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-berman-59) These include the [strictly convex deltahedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deltahedron#Strictly_convex_deltahedron "Deltahedron") whose faces are all equilateral triangles.[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-cundy-60)
- Symmetrohedron is the family of infinitely many highly symmetric convex polyhedra, coined by [Craig S. Kaplan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Craig_S._Kaplan "Craig S. Kaplan") and [George W. Hart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_W._Hart "George W. Hart"), containing regular polygonal faces on symmetry axes with gaps on the convex hull filled by irregular polygons. This involves three-dimensional symmetric groups of tetrahedral, octahedral, and icosahedral symmetry.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-kh-61)
- [Near-miss Johnson solid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Near-miss_Johnson_solid "Near-miss Johnson solid") is a strictly convex polyhedron whose faces are close to being regular polygons, but some or all of which are not precisely regular. Thus, it fails to meet the definition of a Johnson solid, a polyhedron whose faces are all regular, though it "can often be physically constructed without noticing the discrepancy" between its regular and irregular faces.[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-kh-61)
- [Goldberg polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Goldberg_polyhedron "Goldberg polyhedron") is a convex polyhedron with hexagonal and pentagonal faces. Described by [Goldberg (1937)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#CITEREFGoldberg1937), it is obtained from regular octahedron, regular tetrahedron, and regular icosahedron.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-goldberg-62)[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-sg-63)

Convex polyhedra can be categorized into [elementary polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elementary_polyhedra "Elementary polyhedra") or composite polyhedra. Elementary polyhedra are convex, regular-faced polyhedra that cannot be produced into two or more polyhedra by slicing them with a plane.[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartshorne2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidEJCSL9S6la0CpgPA464_464]-64) Quite opposite to composite polyhedra, they can be alternatively defined as polyhedra constructed by attaching more elementary polyhedra. For example, [triaugmented triangular prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triaugmented_triangular_prism "Triaugmented triangular prism") is composite since it can be constructed by attaching three [equilateral square pyramids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equilateral_square_pyramid "Equilateral square pyramid") onto the square faces of a [triangular prism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Triangular_prism "Triangular prism"); the square pyramids and the triangular prism are elementaries.[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-timofeenko-2010-65)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Midsphere.png/250px-Midsphere.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Midsphere.png)

A midsphere

Some convex polyhedra possess a [midsphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Midsphere "Midsphere"), a sphere [tangent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tangent "Tangent") to each of their edges, which is intermediate in radius between the [insphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insphere "Insphere") and [circumsphere](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Circumsphere "Circumsphere") for polyhedra in which all these spheres are present. Every convex polyhedron is combinatorially equivalent to a _canonical polyhedron_, a polyhedron that has a midsphere whose center coincides with the [centroid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centroid "Centroid") of its tangent points with edges. The shape of the canonical polyhedron (but not its scale or position) is uniquely determined by the combinatorial structure of the given polyhedron.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-66)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Herschel_graph_LS.svg/250px-Herschel_graph_LS.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Herschel_graph_LS.svg)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Herschel_enneahedron_animated.gif/250px-Herschel_enneahedron_animated.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Herschel_enneahedron_animated.gif)

[Herschel graph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herschel_graph "Herschel graph") is [planar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planar_graph "Planar graph") and [three-connected](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_connectivity "Vertex connectivity"), satisfying [Steinitz's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steinitz%27s_theorem "Steinitz's theorem").[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-clp-67) This results in a convex [enneahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enneahedron "Enneahedron") with nine quadrilateral faces.[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-coxeter-68)

By forgetting the face structure, any polyhedron gives rise to a [graph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graph_\(discrete_mathematics\) "Graph (discrete mathematics)"), called the [skeleton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Graph_of_a_polytope "Graph of a polytope") of the polyhedron, with corresponding vertices and edges. Such figures have a long history: [Leonardo da Vinci](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonardo_da_Vinci "Leonardo da Vinci") devised frame models of the regular solids, which he drew for [Pacioli](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pacioli "Pacioli")'s book _Divina Proportione_, and similar [wire-frame](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wire-frame_model "Wire-frame model") polyhedra appear in [M.C. Escher](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M.C._Escher "M.C. Escher")'s print [_Stars_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stars_\(M._C._Escher\) "Stars (M. C. Escher)").[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-69) One highlight of this approach is [Steinitz's theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steinitz%27s_theorem "Steinitz's theorem"), which gives a purely graph-theoretic characterization of the skeletons of convex polyhedra: it states that the skeleton of every convex polyhedron is a [planar graph](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Planar_graph "Planar graph") with [three-connected](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vertex_connectivity "Vertex connectivity"), and every such graph is the skeleton of some convex polyhedron. In other words, these are the connected graphs that can be drawn in the plane without any edges crossing, and that stay connected after removing any two of its vertices.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGr%C3%BCnbaum2003235%E2%80%93244-70)

Prominent non-convex polyhedra include the [star polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Star_polyhedra "Star polyhedra"). The regular star polyhedra, also known as the [Kepler–Poinsot polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kepler%E2%80%93Poinsot_polyhedra "Kepler–Poinsot polyhedra"), are constructible via [stellation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stellation "Stellation") or [faceting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faceting "Faceting") of regular convex polyhedra. Stellation is the process of extending the faces (within their planes) so that they meet. Faceting is the process of removing parts of a polyhedron to create new faces (or facets) without creating any new vertices.[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-bridge-71)[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-72) A facet of a polyhedron is any polygon whose corners are vertices of the polyhedron, and is not a _[face](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Face_\(geometry\) "Face (geometry)")_;[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-bridge-71) for example, a polygon involving diagonals ([face diagonals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Face_diagonal "Face diagonal") or [space diagonals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space_diagonal "Space diagonal")). The stellation and faceting are inverse or reciprocal processes: the dual of some stellation is a faceting of the dual to the original polyhedron.[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-73) Other examples are the [Chazelle polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chazelle_polyhedron "Chazelle polyhedron")[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-74) and toroidal polyhedra.[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-75)

## Other families of polyhedra

### Space-filling polyhedra

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Five_parallelohedron_with_colorful_edges.svg/250px-Five_parallelohedron_with_colorful_edges.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Five_parallelohedron_with_colorful_edges.svg)

Paralellohedra, one of special kinds of space-filling polyhedra

Main articles: [Space-filling polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space-filling_polyhedron "Space-filling polyhedron") and [Honeycomb (geometry)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Honeycomb_\(geometry\) "Honeycomb (geometry)")

A [space-filling polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Space-filling_polyhedron "Space-filling polyhedron") is one that can be packed together with copies of itself to completely fill space. Such a close-packing or space-filling is often called a _tessellation_ of space or a _honeycomb_. These include the [parallelohedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parallelohedra "Parallelohedra") (or Federov polyhedra), each of which forms a tessellation using copies of itself translated without rotation;[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-76) the [plesiohedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plesiohedra "Plesiohedra"), defined as the [Voronoi cells](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voronoi_cell "Voronoi cell") of symmetric [Delone sets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Delone_set "Delone set"); and the [Hill tetrahedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hill_tetrahedra "Hill tetrahedra"), a family of space-filling tetrahedra. Parallelohedra and plesiohedra are examples of [stereohedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stereohedra "Stereohedra"), which tile space isohedrally.[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-77) Every space-filling polyhedron must have its [Dehn invariant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dehn_invariant "Dehn invariant") equal to zero.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-78)

Some honeycombs involve more than one kind of polyhedron, such as a [tiling of octahedra and tetrahedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tetrahedral-octahedral_honeycomb "Tetrahedral-octahedral honeycomb") and [octahedra and cuboctahedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rectified_cubic_honeycomb "Rectified cubic honeycomb").[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-79)

### Flexible polyhedra

Main article: [Flexible polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flexible_polyhedron "Flexible polyhedron")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Ste-anim.gif/250px-Ste-anim.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ste-anim.gif)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Br1-anim.gif/250px-Br1-anim.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Br1-anim.gif)

[Steffen's polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steffen%27s_polyhedron "Steffen's polyhedron") and one of the [Bricard polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bricard_octahedron "Bricard octahedron")'s type

Some polyhedra can change their overall shape, while keeping the shapes of their faces the same, by varying the angles of their edges. A polyhedron that can do this is called a flexible polyhedron. By [Cauchy's rigidity theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cauchy%27s_theorem_\(geometry\) "Cauchy's theorem (geometry)"), flexible polyhedra must be non-convex. The volume of a flexible polyhedron must remain constant as it flexes; this result is known as the bellows theorem.[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-80) Three examples are [Steffen's polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Steffen%27s_polyhedron "Steffen's polyhedron"),[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-81) [Bricard octahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bricard_octahedron "Bricard octahedron"),[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-82) and [Kokotsakis polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kokotsakis_polyhedron "Kokotsakis polyhedron").[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-83)

### Ideal polyhedron

Main article: [Ideal polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_polyhedron "Ideal polyhedron")

Convex polyhedra can be defined in three-dimensional [hyperbolic space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hyperbolic_space "Hyperbolic space") in the same way as in Euclidean space, as the [convex hulls](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_hull "Convex hull") of finite sets of points. However, in hyperbolic space, it is also possible to consider [ideal points](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_point "Ideal point") and the points within the space. An [ideal polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_polyhedron "Ideal polyhedron") is the convex hull of a finite set of ideal points.[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-thurston-84) Its faces are ideal polygons, but its edges are defined by entire hyperbolic lines rather than line segments, and its vertices (the ideal points of which it is the convex hull) do not lie within the hyperbolic space.

### Lattice polyhedron

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Tetraedro_de_Reeve.gif/250px-Tetraedro_de_Reeve.gif)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Tetraedro_de_Reeve.gif)

Reeve tetrahedra for different choices of the parameter

Convex polyhedra in which all vertices have integer coordinates are called [lattice polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Convex_lattice_polytope "Convex lattice polytope") or [integral polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral_polyhedron "Integral polyhedron"). The [Ehrhart polynomial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ehrhart_polynomial "Ehrhart polynomial") of lattice a polyhedron counts how many points with [integer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer "Integer") coordinates lie within a scaled copy of the polyhedron, as a function of the scale factor. The study of these polynomials lies at the intersection of [combinatorics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Combinatorics "Combinatorics") and [commutative algebra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commutative_algebra "Commutative algebra").[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-stanley-97-85) An example is [Reeve tetrahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reeve_tetrahedron "Reeve tetrahedron").[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-k-86)

There is a far-reaching equivalence between lattice polyhedra and certain [algebraic varieties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algebraic_variety "Algebraic variety") called [toric varieties](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toric_variety "Toric variety").[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-87) This was used by Stanley to prove the [Dehn–Sommerville equations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dehn%E2%80%93Sommerville_equations "Dehn–Sommerville equations") for [simplicial polytopes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simplicial_polytope "Simplicial polytope").[[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-stanley-96-88)

### Polyhedral compound

A [polyhedral compound](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedral_compound "Polyhedral compound") is made of two or more polyhedra sharing a common centre. Symmetrical compounds often share the same vertices as other well-known polyhedra and may often also be formed by stellation. Some are listed in the [list of Wenninger polyhedron models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/List_of_Wenninger_polyhedron_models "List of Wenninger polyhedron models").

### Zonohedron

A [zonohedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zonohedron "Zonohedron") is a convex polyhedron in which every face is a [polygon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polygon "Polygon") that is symmetric under [rotations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rotation "Rotation") through 180°. Zonohedra can also be characterized as the [Minkowski sums](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Minkowski_sum "Minkowski sum") of line segments, and include several important space-filling polyhedra.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-89)

### Orthogonal polyhedron

Main article: [Orthogonal polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthogonal_polyhedron "Orthogonal polyhedron")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Soma_cube_figures.svg/250px-Soma_cube_figures.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Soma_cube_figures.svg)

Some [orthogonal polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthogonal_polyhedra "Orthogonal polyhedra") made of [Soma cube](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Soma_cube "Soma cube") pieces, themselves [polycubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polycube "Polycube")

Polyhedra are said to be [orthogonal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthogonal_polyhedra "Orthogonal polyhedra") because all of their edges are parallel to the axes of a Cartesian coordinate system.[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-90) This implies that all faces meet at [right angles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Right_angle "Right angle"), but this condition is weaker: [Jessen's icosahedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jessen%27s_icosahedron "Jessen's icosahedron") has faces meeting at right angles, but does not have axis-parallel edges.[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-91) Aside from the [rectangular cuboids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rectangular_cuboid "Rectangular cuboid"), orthogonal polyhedra are nonconvex. They are the three-dimensional analogs of two-dimensional orthogonal polygons, also known as [rectilinear polygons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rectilinear_polygon "Rectilinear polygon"). Orthogonal polyhedra are used in [computational geometry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computational_geometry "Computational geometry"), where their constrained structure has enabled advances in problems unsolved for arbitrary polyhedra, for example, unfolding the surface of a polyhedron to a [polygonal net](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polygonal_net "Polygonal net").[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-92) [Polycubes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polycube "Polycube") are a special case of orthogonal polyhedra that can be decomposed into identical cubes, and are three-dimensional analogues of planar [polyominoes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyomino "Polyomino").[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-93)

## Generalisations

The name 'polyhedron' has come to be used for a variety of objects having similar structural properties to traditional polyhedra.

### Apeirohedra

A classical polyhedral surface has a finite number of faces, joined in pairs along edges. The [apeirohedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apeirohedron "Apeirohedron") form a related class of objects with infinitely many faces. Examples of apeirohedra include:

- tilings or [tessellations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tessellation "Tessellation") of the plane, and
- sponge-like structures called [infinite skew polyhedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skew_apeirohedron "Skew apeirohedron").

### Complex polyhedra

Main article: [Complex polytope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_polytope "Complex polytope")

There are objects called complex polyhedra, for which the underlying space is a [complex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number "Complex number") [Hilbert space](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert_space "Hilbert space") rather than real Euclidean space. Precise definitions exist only for the regular complex polyhedra, whose symmetry groups are [complex reflection groups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_reflection_group "Complex reflection group"). The complex polyhedra are mathematically more closely related to [configurations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Configuration_\(polytope\) "Configuration (polytope)") than to real polyhedra.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-94)

### Curved polyhedra

Some fields of study allow polyhedra to have curved faces and edges. Curved faces can allow [digonal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digon "Digon") faces to exist with a positive area.

- When the surface of a sphere is divided by finitely many [great arcs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_arc "Great arc") (equivalently, by planes passing through the center of the sphere), the result is called a [spherical polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spherical_polyhedron "Spherical polyhedron"). Many convex polytopes having some degree of symmetry (for example, all the Platonic solids) can be projected onto the surface of a concentric sphere to produce a spherical polyhedron. However, the reverse process is not always possible; some spherical polyhedra (such as the [hosohedra](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hosohedron "Hosohedron")) have no flat-faced analogue.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-95)
- If faces are allowed to be concave as well as convex, adjacent faces may be made to meet together with no gap. Some of these curved polyhedra can pack together to fill space. Two important types are bubbles in froths and foams such as [Weaire-Phelan bubbles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weaire%E2%80%93Phelan_structure "Weaire–Phelan structure"),[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-96) and forms used in architecture.[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-97)

## Higher-dimensional polyhedra

Main article: [n-dimensional polyhedron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N-dimensional_polyhedron "N-dimensional polyhedron")

From the latter half of the twentieth century, various mathematical constructs have been found to have properties also present in traditional polyhedra. Rather than confining the term "polyhedron" to describe a three-dimensional polytope, it has been adopted to describe various related but distinct kinds of structure.

A polyhedron has been defined as a set of points in [real](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Real_number "Real number") [affine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Affine_space "Affine space") (or [Euclidean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclidean_space "Euclidean space")) space of any dimension _n_ that has flat sides. It may alternatively be defined as the intersection of finitely many [half-spaces](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Half-space_\(geometry\) "Half-space (geometry)"). Unlike a conventional polyhedron, it may be bounded or unbounded. In this meaning, a [polytope](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polytope "Polytope") is a bounded polyhedron.[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-polytope-bounded-1-16)[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-polytope-bounded-2-17)

Analytically, such a convex polyhedron is expressed as the solution set for a system of linear inequalities. Defining polyhedra in this way provides a geometric perspective for problems in [linear programming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_programming "Linear programming").[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polyhedron#cite_note-98)
